UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The Government of Upper Volta has requested IDA to continue its
assistance in the financing of the Rural Development Fund. This report
appraises the Second Rural Development Fund Project, a continuation of
the project financed by Credit 317-UV, which has been successful in
financing small agricultural and rural development subprojects. An IDA
Credit of US$9.4 million is proposed. The project was prepared by RMWA
in April 1975 and appraised by an IDA mission in October 1975.
ii.       Upper Volta is one of the least developed countries.  It has a
traditional and overwhelmingly rural economy. Farming is difficult due
to erratic rainfall and a prolonged dry season, poor and shallow soils,
and farming systems that generally have not evolved beyond shifting cul-
tivation with little use of cash inputs.   Consequently, productivity is
very low, and while agriculture and livestock provide a living for 96% of
the population, they generate less than 45% of GDP. Approximately 2.2
million hectares are under cultivation, and grains (the basic food crops
are millet, sorghum and maize) represent 80% of agricultural production.
The most striking feature is the sharp regional contrast. In the central
and northern regions farming is at subsistence level due to high popula-
tion, poor soils and irregular rainfall, and this is particularly so in
the case of the Mossi plateau. Conversely, in the western and south-
western regions, production is more diversified and market-oriented, as
growing conditions and soils are more favorable and population density
is lower.
iii.      The First Rural Development Fund Project (RDF I) was appraised
in August 1971, signed in June 1972, and became effective in October 1972.
Progress was slow through 1973 due to delays in the recruitment of staff,
delays in Government arranging for prefinancing of project expenditures,
and slow procurement of equipment. By 1974, however, the project gained
momentum, which accelerated through 1975. Project funds have been depleted
due to the dollar devaluation, which reduced IDA's contribution to an equiv-
alent of about 86% of the original estimate, and inflation, which was much
higher than estîmated at appraisal.
iv.       The proposed Second Rural Development Fund Project (RDF II)
will, with few exceptions, be concentrated on the Mossi plateau for the
following reasons: (a) the ability of the Rural Development Fund (Fonds
de Developpement Rural - FDR) to finance a variety of small-scale projects
is best utilized under Mossi plateau conditions where there is little
potential for major agricultural development projects; (b) major agricul-
tural development projects are being implemented in other regions; and



- ii -
(c) such concentration would be in line with Government rural development
strategy.
v.        The project provides basically a line of credit to FDR for invest-
ments in small-scale rural projects. The main purposes of the subprojects
would be to increase crop production and improve village water supplies.
The project would be carried out over five years (1975/76 through 1979/80);
but most of the activities would be undertaken during the last four years,
with 1975/76 being treated as a bridging year, financed partly under Credit
317-UV and partly under RDF II. Based on past experience and assessments
made by FDR and other agencies, the program of activities for RDF II would
be roughly as listed below; project cost calculations are based on a program
of this content:
(a) bottomland development, 2,700 ha;
(b) erosion control works, 9,200 ha;
(c) small-scale irrigation and improved bottomland development,
500 ha;
(d) wells, 520;
(e) tubewells, 180;
(f) village warehouses, 400, and village centers, 20;
(g) medium-term credit for agricultural equipment, US$0.5
million equivalent;
(h) provision for unidentified projects, US$0.6 million;
(i) studies for economic development of areas freed from
onchocerciasis.
In support of the above subprojects, the following would also be financed
under the project:
(j) maintenance brigade for tubewells;
(k) staff, equipment and operating expenditure incurred by FDR
in executing the project, and some support for the Rural
Development Works Department (HAER) and the Regional
Development Organizations (ORDs);
(1) training of Voltaic staff;
(m) expatriate technical assistance to help in project execution.



- iii -
Although project costs reflect the above development program, the physical
targets are not absolute and flexibility would be maintained throughout the
project period to enable emphasis to be shifted as and when required.
vi.       An internationally-supported riverblindness eradication campaign
has been implemented in the valleys of the Volta since 1974. The effect
of control spraying is almost immediate although efforts need to be sus-
tained for 20 years to ensure permanent control, but in a few areas people
are already returning to the fertile valleys in expectation of permanent
control. These valleys constitute one of West Africa's last remaining
natural resources and it is imperative that optimal use be made of them
and that land development be controlled. Therefore the project includes
studies for economic development of areas freed from onchocerciasis (oncho
studies) which would involve preparation of:
(a) an inventory of resources;
(b) a development plan which would identify priorities for
development in the oncho areas; and
(c) a number of specific investment proposals within the
framework of the development plan.
Overall responsibility for the oncho studies would rest with the existing
Coordinating Committee for Rural Development; however, specific ministries
would execute the various studies: the Ministry of Rural Development would
be responsible for the inventory of resources and feasibility studies, and
the Ministry of Planning for the development plan. For practical purposes
disbursements would be handled through FDR.
vii.      Investment requirements of the Fund over the five-year period
(1975/76 - 1979/80) are estimated at US$16.2 million. An IDA credit of
US$9.4 million would finance 62% of project costs net of taxes. The re-
mainder of the project costs would be financed as follows: Banque Arabe
pour le Developpement Economique en Afrique (BADEA), US$4.5 million (30%
of project costs net of taxes); Fonds d'Aide et de Cooperation (FAC), US$0.6
million (4%); Dutch Government, US$0.15 million (1%); United Nations Develop-
ment Program (UNDP), US$0.45 million (3%); and Government, US$1.1 million (7%
of project costs equivalent to estimated taxes).
viii.     The IDA credit would be on standard terms; the BADEA loan would
be for 25 years with 5 years grace and an interest rate to be set between
2% and 4%. The UNDP, FAC, and Dutch Government contributions would be
grants. All cofinancing would be on a parallel basis. BADEA's contribution
would be for the wells, tubewells, maintenance brigade for tubewells, and
related supporting services. Contributions of FAC, the Dutch Government and
UNDP would be for expatriate technical assistance. As funds available under
Credit 317-UV are likely to be exhausted before funds from the RDF Il credit
become available, retroactive financing of up to US$350,000 is proposed. It
is recognized that the cofinancing will result in procurement and disbursement



- iv -
procedures which will vary among the various agencies. For BADEA's contri-
bution of US$4.5 million, procurement would be in accordance with BADEA
rules. These are not expected to involve Government in higher costs than
if the project were financed only by IDA. The services of expatriate
staff and volunteers valued at about US$1.2 million, financed by UNDP,
FAC and the Dutch Government, would be obtained in accordance with these
agencies' procedures.
ix.       Items financed under the IDA credit would be procured as follows:
vehicles, tractors, other equipment and supplies such as cement and steel,
valued at US$2.0 million, would be obtained through international competi-
tive bidding in accordance with IDA guidelines in the case of contracts
over US$50,000, and through local competitive bidding in the case of con-
tracts of less than US$50,000. For contracts under US$10,000, direct com-
petitive purchasing would be employed. Domestically manufactured goods
would be allowed a 15% preference or the applicable import duty, which-
ever is lower. A large part of project costs would be for labor, sal-
aries, operating and training expenses, valued at about US$4.2 million,
none of which are suitable for competitive bidding. The services of con-
sultants and expatriate staff valued at about US$1.4 million would be ob-
tained according to procedures acceptable to IDA. These services are
mainly for the oncho studies and total cost per consultant employed has
been estimated at US$7,500 equivalent per man-month, made up as follows:
average consultant fee and daily allowance, US$6,000; international and
local travel, US$1,000; and US$500 for miscellaneous items. A total amount
of US$1.1 million would be for agricultural credit and unidentified proj-
ects. An amount of US$1.8 million would cover contingencies.
x.        The IDA credit would be disbursed as follows:  Category I - all
project works and supporting services for the wells, tubewells, and tube-
well maintenance brigade components of the project, 89% of total expendi-
ture, amounting to US$200,000; Category II (all project works and sup-
porting services except those in Category I) - (a) imported goods and
foreign consultants' services, 100% of foreign expenditures, amounting to
US$1,200,000; (b) other eligible expenditures, 89% of total expenditures,
amounting to US$5,000,000; Category III - studies for economic develop-
ment of areas freed from onchocerciasis: (a) internationally recruited
staff, 100% of total expenditures, amounting to US$160,000; (b) consul-
tancy services, 100% of foreign expenditures amounting to US$1,140,000;
(c) other expenditures for vehicles, operating costs and local counter-
part staff, 80% of total expenditures, amounting to US$100,000; and Cate-
gory IV - unallocated, US$1,600,000. Retroactive financing by IDA would
be permitted under Categories I and II for up to US$350,000 and IDA financ-
ing under Category I would be terminated on the day of signing of the BADEA
agreement. BADEA would not provide retroactive financing and its contri-
bution would be disbursed against 100% of total expenditures for wells,
tubewells, the maintenance brigade for tubewells and related supporting
services. This would amount to US$3.4 million, and US$1.1 mitlion would



v -
be unallocated. FAC, the Dutch Government, and UNDP would disburse directly
in accordance with their own procedures. IDA disbursements would be against
certified records of expenditures and import documentation.
xi.       The Rural Development Fund (RDF) will continue as now to be ulti-
mately responsible for project execution. RDF staff will be strengthened
and the Interministerial Technical Committee established for RDF I will be
changed and its membership reduced to improve efficiency. The Regional De-
velopment Organizations (ORDs) and the Rural Development Works Department
(HAER) will be responsible for identification, execution, and postinvest-
ment implementation. RDF will be responsible for appraisal, end-inspection,
and evaluation. RDF's evaluation function will be considerably strengthened
under the project.
xii.      The main impact of the productive subcomponent of the project would
be additional production of cereals such as millet and sorghum, and paddy.
If the assumed development plan materializes, ineremental annual production
at full development in 1980 is estimated at 1,200 and 5,300 tons respectively.
This will not create any marketing problems.
xiii.     Farmers would benefit from increased crop production, better stor-
age of crops and inputs and improved water supplies. Improvements in income
enjoyed by participating farm families in productive subprojects are expected
to average about CFAF 13,000 (US$60) for erosion control; CFAF 9,800 (US$45)
in the case of bottomland development; CFAF 14,700 (US$67) for improved
bottomland development; and about CFAF 27,000 (US$120) for small-scale irri-
gation. Farmers taking up animal-drawn cultivation financed by medium-term
credit provided by the project would benefit from saved man-days that would
allow an expansion in the area they cultivate. Through this, incremental
income could amount to as much as CFAF 40,000 (US$175) per family. It is
estimated that about 15,000 farm families would benefit from the produc-
tive subprojects, and many more people from the wells and village build-
ings constructed.
xiv.      The project has important social and institutional benefits.  The
village wells provide an assured water supply to populations who otherwise
are forced to depend on small and often distant creeks, which may run dry
for as much as three months of the year and which often are a health hazard.
Institutional strengthening has already been achieved under the first project
and this would again be reinforced under this follow-up project. Some of
the subprojects are directly productive while others have large social and
health benefits which are difficult to quantify. The project provides a line
of credit for investment in various small-scale subprojects. Rate of return
calculations are therefore only tentative. However, if the mix of projects
set out in para v materialized, the investment package provided under RDF Il
would obtain a rate of return of 16%. Under the very difficult ecological
and human conditions of the plateau, rates in excess of 10% must be consid-
ered quite satisfactory. Furthermore, the rate of return fails to reflect
an important aspect, namely that the benefits accrue to the poorest segments
of Upper Volta's population.






UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
1. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Upper Volta has asked IDA to provide additional
finance for its Rural Development Fund. The Fund was established in 1972
with funds provided under IDA Credit 317-UV. This report appraises a Second
Rural Development Fund Project (RDF II) and recommends a second IDA Credit
for the Fund. The Rural Development Fund Project (RDF 1) has been success-
ful (details. are in Chapter III) in financing small and scattered agricul-
tural and rural development subprojects. This approach would continue under
RDF II.
1.02      The proposed project would be the sixth IDA credit for the agri-
cultural sector in Upper Volta. Previous agricultural projects are the
West Volta Cotton Project (1970, Credit 225-UV, US$6.2 million), the Rural
Development Fund Project (1972, Credit 317-UV, US$2.2 million), the Drought
Relief Fund Project (1973, Credit 442-UV, US$2.0 million), the Bougouriba
Agricultural Development Project (1974, Credit 496-UV, US$8.0 million),
and the Livestock Development Project (1975, Credit 557-UV, US$9.0 mil-
lion). Other projects which are also important for the rural sector are
the Education Project (1973, Credit 430-UV, US$2.85 million) which has a
substantial rural training component, and the recently signed Rural Roads
Project (1975, Credit 579-UV, US$7.5 million).
1.03      An identification report was submitted by the Director of the
Rural Development Fund early in 1975. The project was prepared by an RMWA
mission in April 1975, and appraised by an IDA mission composed of M4essrs.
R. van der Lugt, J. L. Ginnsz (IDA), V. Fudzie (ADB), B. Victor-Pujebet,
and H. Wissink (Consultants), which visited Upper Volta in October 1975.
II. BACKGROUND
A Rural Economy
2.01      Upper Volta, with an area of 274,000 km   and a population of 5.6
million, is classified by the United Nations as one of the 25 "least devel-
oped countries". In 1974 average GDP per capita barely exceeded US$80. It
has a traditional and overwhelmingly rural economy: 99% of the labor force
is composed of self-employed farmers and pastoralists and their families.
Farming is difficult because of erratic rainfall and a prolonged dry season,
poor and shallow soils, and farming systems that generally have not evolved
beyond shifting cultivation with little use of cash inputs. Consequently
productivity is very low in the agricultural sector, and while agriculture
and livestock provide a living for 96% of the population, they generate less
than 45% of GDP.



-2-
2.02      Crop farming provides a living for almost 90% of the population,
and accounts for about 31% of GDP and 45% of total export earnings. By
contrast, livestock employs only 6% of the population but produces a rela-
tively high share of GDP (10-12%), and provides about one third to one half
of export earnings. However, in spite of the predominantly rural economy,
foodgrain imports are important. They represent about 20% of total import
value in normal years and substantially more when drought occurs. Average
imports, 1969-71, amounted to some 40,000 tons of grain. Of these about
26,000 tons were wheat and flour and wheat cannot be grown in Upper Volta
except with irrigation. At the height of the Sahelian drought as much as
100,000 tons of grain imports were required annually.
2.03      The 1971-73 drought caused economie growth in Upper Volta first to
stagnate, and then to decline. This disruption followed an already low rate
of growth in the preceding six years. In addition, the drought exacerbated
the existing disparities between the densely populated central plateau lands
(Mossi plateau) and the western, southwestern and, to a lesser extent, east-
ern regions of the country which, because of riverblindness, are less popu-
lated but have more fertile soils and generally higher rainfall.
2.04      Government has substantial external reserves and a high level
of deposits with the internal banking system. This reflects the austerity
policy which Government introduced in 1968 to correct budget deficits.
This entailed strict expenditure control, from which public services con-
cerned with economic development suffered most. The operating budget for
agriculture and livestock services combined amounted to only 5% of total
current expenditures in 1973. The largest item of current expenditure is
education, about 25%. The budgets of the eleven Regional Development Or-
ganizations (see para 2.08) in charge of rural development stagnated over
the 1969-73 period, and decreased in real terms; although they were in-
creased by about 10% in 1974, this was not enough to compensate for in-
creased wages and operating costs. Thus, shortage of operating funds--in
spite of a comparatively strong Government financial position--has been a
major constraint on development and there is a need to adjust Government
expenditure priorities if a strong rural development effort is to be
supported., Government granted wage increases varying between 20% and
50% as of January 1976, and this might further constrain ORD operating
budgets.
Agriculture
2.05      Approximately 2.2 million ha are under cultivation and cereals
represent 80% of agricultural production. Agricultural statistics are
in Annex 1. The most striking feature is the sharp regional contrast:
in the central and northern regions, crop farming, basically the produc-
tion of sorghum, millet and groundnuts, is at subsistence level due to
high population, poor soils and irregular rainfall. Conversely, in the
western and southwestern regions production is more diversified and
market oriented, as growing conditions and soils are more favorable and
population density is lower.



-3-
2.06.     The basic food crops are millet, sorghum and maize, of which little
is marketed. Rice, sweet potatoes, cotton, groundnuts, sesame and sheanuts
are marketed in greater quantities for local consumption or for export.  A
traditional system of shifting cultivation is used everywhere, but in areas
where population pressure is high, such as the Mossi plateau, soils have
deteriorated because of an insufficient fallow period. Cotton, the most
important cash crop and a major export, is grown mainly in the western and
southwestern regions.
2.07      Livestock is one of the country's principal exports.  Annual milk
and meat production are estimated at 100,000 tons and 54,000 tons respectively,
and average domestic per capita meat consumption at 8.6 kg, half of which
is beef. On the Mossi plateau farmers are increasingly turning to fattening
cattle with crop residues and by-products. Most farmers keep snall stock,
goats, sheep, pigs and poultry around their houses where they live by scav-
enging.
2.08      The country is divided into Regional Development Organizations
(Organisme Regional de Developpement - ORD), public agencies administered by
boards of directors representing local administtation and population. Except
for the valleys of the Volta, ORDs are responsible for planning and implement-
ing all agricultural operations in their region; they are supervised by the
Ministry of Rural Development. An autonomous Volta Valleys Development
Authority (Autorite pour l'Amenagement des Vallees des Volta - AVV) is re-
sponsible for all development activities in the valleys of the Volta. AVV
is also supervised by the Ministry of Rural Development. Agricultural re-
search is carried out by specialized French institutions. By and large,
research has produced good technology packages for cotton, groundnuts,
rice, vegetables and, to a lesser extent, maize. An effective package
has yet to be developed, however, for the traditional staples, millet and
sorghum.
2.09      Mlarketing is mainly in the hands of private traders, but Govern-
ment involvement is increasing. Cotton, sesame and sheanut exports are
controlled by a Government agency, the Stabilization Fund, and official
minimum producer prices are set. In addition, reference export prices are
announced and if actual prices obtained are higher than the reference price,
the difference is paid to the Stabilization Fund; where actual prices are
lower, the exporters receive the difference from the Stabilization Fund.
Compagnie Francaise pour le Developpment des Fibres Textiles (CFDT) has
a monopoly on cotton exports, and is presently the largest contributor to
Stabilization Fund surpluses. Vegetable exports are handled through a pro-
ducers' cooperative which has not been very successful in domestic market-
ing, but which has developed an air freight market for high quality exports
to Europe and the Ivory Coast. To improve cereal marketing and stabilize
domestic prices, OFNACER (Office National des Cereales) was created in 1971,
but has mainly been involved with distributing relief foodgrains.  In 1974/75,
Government set up an official grain marketing svstem through ORDs and OFNACER,
but this has not been very effective.



-4-
Rural Development Strategy
2.10      A recent Bank report on the economic situation in Upper Volta 1/
examines the long-term development perspectives. These can be summarized as
follows: (a) the Mossi plateau is heavily populated; in the five ORDs cover-
ing the plateau 2/, 61% of the population lives on 34% of the available land,
and in many districts population density exceeds 40 inhabitants per sq km,
which is too high to maintain even moderately productive systems of agricul-
ture under presently available techniques. Efforts over the past 15 years
to improve living standards of the local population have failed. Only a
reduction in population levels could increase land availability and improve
incomes; and (b) Upper Volta has large areas which are still relatively
underpopulated or even empty, and which, because they have good agricul-
tural potential, could support satisfactory standards of living. People
could be attracted to these areas through settlement projects, integrated
rural development projects and possibly irrigation schemes. Many of these
potential settlement areas, however, are in the valleys of the Black, Red
and White Volta Rivers where onchocerciasis (riverblindness) has been en-
demic.
2.11      Settlement in the valleys of the Voltas depends on the eradica-
tion of riverblindness. An internationally supported eradication campaign
was started in 1974 under which about 1,600,000 ha would be freed from river-
blindness over a 20-year period; of this, an estimated 600,000 ha would be
suitable for agriculture.
2.12      Intensive settlement could be possible through developing irriga-
tion for which a potential of some 130,000 ha is estimated to exist. How-
ever, given the high investment cost of irrigation and the severe lack of
experienced personnel, it would appear advisable to concentrate initially
on rainfed agriculture.
2.13      While the scope for a large-scale agricultural development program
capable of bringing about meaningful improvements in the income of any sig-
nificant number of people on the Mossi plateau does not appear to exist,
there is both the opportunity and need for implementing a variety of small-
scale projects designed either to take advantage of limited areas of land
of good potential, for example bottomlands, or to satisfy the needs of rural
communities, such as wells and village warehouses. Such opportunities have
been successfully exploited under RDF I and this would be continued under
the proposed second project.
2.14      In sum, the Bank mission concluded that Government's principal
effort should be to develop the west and southwest where conditions could
support reasonably productive farming systems, and to implement a program
1/   Report No. 564a-UV, dated July 7, 1975, Current Economic Position and
Development Prospects of Upper Volta.
2/   Ouagadougou, Yatenga, Kaya, Koudougou and Koupela.



- 5 -
of small-scale improvements on the Mossi plateau where needed by the local
communities and where economically viable. In general, Government has
adopted this approach.
III. RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND I
3.01      The First Rural Development Fund Project (RDF I) was appraised
in August 1971; Credit 317-UV was signed in June 1972 and became effective
in October 1972. The objective of the Fund (see also para 5.01) was to
finance small-scale rural development activities designed to increase agri-
cultural production and to improve the health and well-being of rural com-
munities which, because of their small size and scattered location, could
not be handled in the context of a traditional Bank/IDA agricultural devel-
opment project. At appraisal it was assumed for calculation purposes that
the project would comprise: (a) 500 wells for domestic water supplies;
(b) 700 village warehouses for storing farm inputs; (c) 1,500 ha of bottom-
land development for rice production; (d) 1,000 ha of erosion control works
to improve yields of traditional crops such as millet, sorghum, cotton and
groundnuts; (e) 200 ha of small irrigation schemes below existing dams for
rice and vegetable production; (f) 270 km of rural roads; and (h) a number
of unidentified projects. Total project costs were estimated at US$3.04
million, and were financed by IDA, US$2.2 million (72% of project costs);
French bilateral aid, US$0.38 million (13%); and Government, US$0.46 mil-
lion (15%).
3.02      The main institutions involved in executing the project were, and
continue to be, the Rural Development Fund (Fonds de Developpement Rural -
FDR), a unit established within the National Development Bank (Banque Na-
tionale de Developpement - BND) for the purpose of managing the Fund and
to appraise the suitability of subprojects for financing; the Rural Devel-
opment Works Department (Direction Hydraulique et Amenagement de l'Espace
Rural - HAER) of the Ministry of Rural Development, responsible for design-
ing and constructing subprojects; and the ORDs, responsible for identifying
and preparing potential subprojects and for their subsequent management.
An Interministerial Technical Committee (Comite Technique Interministriel)
was established to supervise FDR and to approve FDR programs. Another re-
lated institution is the Ministry of Rural Development 1/ which supervises
the ORDs and AVV. A Coordinating Committee for Rural Development (Cormite
de Coordination du Developpement Rural) has been established to determine
national rural development policy, approve related programs and projects
and ensure necessary harmonization and coordination.
1/   At appraisal there was a Ministry for Planning, Rural Development,
Environment and Tourism, but on February 9, 1976, a new Cabinet was
announced. This resulted in a split into three Ministries: Ministry
of Planning, Ministry of Rural Development, and Ministry of Environ-
ment and Tourism.



- 6 -
3.03      Physical Progress.  The project became operational in October
1972 but progress was slow through 1973 due to delays in the recruitment
of staff needed by PWD and HAER, delays in Government arranging the prefi-
nancing of project expenditures, and slow procurement of equipment. By 1974,
however, the project gained momentum, which accelerated through 1975. Proj-
ect funds will be exhausted in March/April 1976, and it is estimated that by
June 1976 the following program will have been completed:
Estimated     Estimated Achieve-  Percentage of
Objectives    ments at Project    Physical Works
at Appraisal       Completion           Completed
Subprojects
Wells (No)                          500                277                45
Village warehouses (No)             700                677                97
Bottomland development (ha)       1,500             1,141                 76
Erosion control works (ha)        1,000               845                 85
Small irrigation schemes (ha)       200                 68                34
Roads (km)                          270                353               130
Under unidentified projects, the following were financed: (a) eight large
warehouses for ORDs; (b) a feasibility study for irrigation of the Kama-
dena plain; (c) part-financing of a cold storage warehouse; and (d) eleven
livestock pens for smallholders to facilitate the production of farmyard
manure.
3.04      Costs.  The costs of the RDF program have proved higher than expec-
ted at appraisal; in part this is due to a level of worldwide price inflation
that was not expected at appraisal, when price contingencies equivalent to
only 5% of total project costs were employed. This not only affected well
construction costs considerably, but also costs of warehouses and road con-
struction.  However, because of changes in design (less complicated than
envisaged), bottomland development costs were lower than appraisal estimates.
The cost overruns were compounded by dollar devaluation; at appraisal the
IDA credit of US$2.2 million was equivalent to CFAF 562.6 million. In fact
the average rate of exchange was lower and the credit generated only CFAF
485 million, or 13% less than estimated.
3.05      Farmer Support.  An important criterion for the selection of sub-
projects under RDF I was that they were perceived as needed by local people
who were prepared to participate by providing free labor (self-help).
3.06      After hesitant and wait-and-see attitude in the early stage-of
RDF I, farmer response became increasingly favorable as the project gained
momentum and became more widely known in rural areas; labor required for
construction has been voluntarily provided by village communities. For
road construction labor, however, villagers received small bonuses. Given



- 7 -
the prevailing local support, Rural Development Fund Operations can continue.
Farmers are also required, after completion of construction, to provide labor
for maintenance. Experience to date however is too short to make either a
negative or positive assessment of farmer response in this respect. Works
seem generally well maintained, but lack of sufficient ORD support in certain
cases resulted in suboptimal maintenance.
3.07      Problems.  A number of problems, in addition to the initial prob-
lems described in para 3.03, which occurred during implementation of RDF 1,
must be corrected if RDF II is to be fully successful.   Basically, most
problems have stemmed from a lack of suitably experienced staff to conceive,
design, and implement subprojects; deficiencies in organization which led to
inadequate supervision of subprojects in both construction and operational
phases; and from less than optimum support (extension, credit and inputs)
to participants in directly productive subprojects such as bottomland devel-
oprment, small-scale irrigation and erosion control.
3.08      The problem of staff is that there are too few qualified people
in FDR, the ORDs, and HAER. This situation reflects an overriding problem
in Upper Volta. In RDF I staff shortages resulted in the failure of the
admittedly small program of irrigation below existing dams, the unnecessary
failure of some bottomland schemes because of poor design, and the fact that
about 50% of the wells constructed now have to be deepened. The solution
under RDF II is described in Chapter VII and involves the recruitment of
expatriate personnel and a training program for Voltaics.
3.09      The organizational problem is related to the staff issue and is
more complex. FDR has been unable, due to lack of staff, to provide the
supervision and monitoring for the program as a whole that was envisaged
at appraisal. Importantly, FDR has been unable to ensure that the subproj-
ects it finances are being constructed and managed properly or to evaluate
the impact of project actions on participants. Chapter VII describes the
way in which FDR will be strengthened in its evaluation and supervisory
roles.
3.10      Under RDF I responsibility was placed on HAER for the execution
of all works with the exception of roads. The ORDs thereafter took over
the management of the subprojects. HAER suffered from a high staff turn-
over which caused delays in project implementation and resulted in less
than optimal coordination between HAER and the ORDs.  Under RDF II RAER
involvement will be limited to construction of the technically more complex
well and small-scale irrigation subprojects. The ORDs will be responsible
for the construction of all other subprojects, especially the important bot-
tomland and soil erosion control activities. The ORDs will be strengthened
to enable them to fulfill these new roles. Since the ORDs will have respon-
sibility for all phases of the subproject cycle, from identification through
operation, higher standards can be expected.



-8-
3.11      A serious problem that became evident once RDF I got underway in
1974 was that the ORDs were not providing the support services, extension,
credit, improved seed and other input supplies needed to maximize the ben-
efits from FDR investments. As a stop-gap measure IDA agreed in late 1974
to finance 40 farm level extension workers to service RDF subprojects.
Under RDF II the arrangements for providing such support services would
be formalized and made mandatory for all FDR-financed activities.
3.12      Lesser problems have arisen other than those concerning staff
and organization; the steps to be taken under RDF II to resolve these are
described in various sections of the report. Overall, however, RDF I,
despite its deficiencies, has demonstrated that the original concept of
the project was sound. In Upper Volta the project is considered a major
success by officials and farmers alike. More details and tables appear in
Annex 2.
IV. THE PROJECT AREA
Choice of the Project Area
4.01      While RDF I financed subprojects throughout Upper Volta, RDF Il
activities would be concentrated on the Mossi plateau (the five ORDs of
Ouagadougou, Koudougou, Koupela, Kaya and Yatenga). This is because:
(a) FDR's ability to finance a variety of small-scale projects is best
utilized under Mossi plateau conditions, where there is little potential
for major agricultural development projects; (b) major agricultural devel-
opment projects are being implemented in other regions; and (c) such con-
centration would be in line with Government rural development strategy (see
paras 2.10 - 2.14). The Mossi plateau is intersected by some of the Volta
valleys. Development of these valleys is the responsibility of the Volta
Valleys Development Authority (AVV) and RDF II financing would not be ex-
tended to these areas. Exceptions to the above would be well and tubewell
construction, which would be financed by FDR as part of the national program,
and a provision equivalent to not more than 10% of project funds which RDF
would be permitted to employ on high priority projects elsewhere in the
country. During negotiations assurances were obtained from Government to
these effects.
Description of the Project Area
4.02      The climate of the project area is Sudanian.  The Mossi plateau
receives an annual rainfall increasing from 600 mm in the north to 950 mmr
in the south. Rainfall is concentrated (85%) in the four-month period



- 9 -
June-September, when it is often torrential with intermittent dry spells.
There are large fluctuations in annual rainfall, and the date of the begin-
ning of the rains, important for soil preparation and sowing, varies widely
from year to year. Both fluctuations strongly influence production. Average
monthly temperatures are around 20 C; maximum temperatures are around 39 0C in
March, April, and May; and minimum temperatures, around 14 C, occur during
January. Project area statistics are in Annex 3.
4.03      Soils are generally poor with nearly 75% classified as shallow,
gravelly sands of low agricultural potential. About the only merit of these
soils is that they are relatively easy to work; they are used for millet/
sorghum and groundnut production. The remaining soils are the heavier
and deeper soils of low-lying areas, some of which are seasonally flooded.
These soils are used for rice and vegetable production; most of the bottom-
land development under RDF I and proposed for RDF Il would be on soils of
this type.
4.04  2   Population densities on the plateau range from 21 to 74 inhabitants
per km . As a result of these high densities, more than half of all culti-
vated lands is cropped each year. This intensive cropping has led to loss
of soil fertility over much of the plateau. With yields decreasing the Mossi
plateau has become a major source of labor for the neighboring coastal coun-
tries, in particular the Ivory Coast. The exodus is mostly of young people.
Despite the poor potential there are ways in which, on a limited scale,
agricultural productivity could be increased. These include (a) intensifi-
cation of cultivation in depressions through better drainage and water con-
trol; (b) contour bunding of cultivated lands and contour cultivation to
improve water retention and erosion control; (c) irrigation from lakes or
ponds, or from water holes traditionally dug by farmers in bottomlands dur-
ing the dry season for irrigation of high value crops such as tobacco and
vegetables; and (d) introducing livestock into the farming system.
4.05      The project area contains about 440 km of bituminized roads and
about 1,400 km of national gravel roads; there are also about 1,500 km of
secondary roads. Road connections between Ouagadougou, the capital, and
the headquarters of the five ORDs, are more than adequate, and while within
the ORDs there are areas which have poor access, a number of large rural and
secondary road improvement programs are in the pipeline, which should allevi-
ate this situation. Under the Bank's Rural Roads Project, a special unit
(Service Entretien des Routes Secondaires - SERS) is being created which
will be responsible for construction and maintenance of all feeder roads
in Upper Volta.



- 10 -
V. THE PROJECT
Background
5.01      The idea of a Rural Development Fund was a result of the Bank's
first economic mission to Upper Volta in November 1969 1/, which concluded
that:
(a)  there was an obvious need by local communities for small-scale
development actions, which if they could be initiated would
be supported by the local population;
(b)  in the past a number of projects in the rural sector had
failed because of lack of local support;
(c)  foreign aid sources, which provide most of the money for
financial development in Upper Volta, had up to then shown
little interest in financing such small-scale actions;
(d)  as Government had started to decentralize its services by
setting up Regional Development Organizations (ORDs),
agencies now existed capable of identifying and imple-
menting such small projects;
(e)  ORD financing, however, came mainly from aid sources
and was tied to specific programs which did not include
this type of project; and thus
(f)  some form of mechanism to finance such actions was required.
5.02      An IDA appraisal mission pursued the matter further, and Credit
317-UV was approved to finance a Rural Development Fund (RDF I) which
constituted a line of credit to finance small-scale development actions.
The basic arrangement was that the Fund would appraise proposals made to
it by the ORDs as and when suitable small-scale proiects were identified
and prepared. Experience so far has proven that this approach is valid
(see Chapter III), although organizational modifications are needed to
improve execution.  Such modifications would be made under RDF II (see
Chapter VII) and the line of credit approach would be continued.
Summary Description
5.03      The project (RDF II) would involve the implementation of small-scale
rural development activities (subprojects) financed through FDR, the main
1/   See Report AW-19a, The Economic Development of Upper Volta, dated
November 17, 1974, in four volumes.



- il -
purpose of which would be to increase crop production and improve
village water supplies. The project would be carried out over five years
(1975/76 through 1979/80), but most of the activities would be executed over
the last four years; 1975/76 being treated as a bridging year, financed
partly under Credit 317-UV and partly under RDF II.
5.04       Based on past experience and assessments made by FDR, the ORDs
and other agencies, it would appear that the program of activities for RDF
Il would be roughly as listed below and project cost calculatLons are based
on this:
(a)  bottomland development, 2,700 ha;
(b)  erosion control works, 9,200 ha;
(c)  small-scale irrigation and improved bottomland
development, 500 ha;
(d)  wells, 520;
(e)  tubewells, 180;
(f)  village warehouses, 400, and village centers, 20;
(g)  medium-term credit for agricultural eq1ipmient,
,JS$0.5 million equivalent;
(h)  provision for unidentified projects, IJS$0.6 million;
(i)  studies for economic development of areas freed from
onchocerciasis.
In support of the above subprojects the following would also be financed
under the project:
(j)  maintenance brigade for tubewells;
(k)  staff, equipment and operating expendi.tires incurred by
FDR in executing the project, and some support for HAER

- 12 -
5.05      Details concerning land and infrastructure development subprojects
are given in Annex 4 and summary descriptions are below.
Detailed Features
5.06      Bottomland Development.  Bottomlands are small swampy depressions
served by small seasonal streams and which can be developed and used for
paddy cultivation by simple water control works. The project includes
2,700 ha to be developed by the ORDs at an estimated cost of about CFAF
41,700/ha (US$185).
5.07      Erosion Control Works.  Erosion control works involve the con-
struction of contour bunds by tractor-drawn disc. Such bunds reduce run-
off, thereby improving water percolation and reducing the erosive effect of
rainfall. As the ORDs have demonstrated their capability for this work, a
much larger program has been provided for than under RDF I. ORDs would be
responsible for preparing and executing about 9,200 ha at an estimated cost
of CFAF 14,740/ha (US$65).
5.08      Small-Scale Irrigation and Improved Bottomland Development.  A
variety of small-scale irrigation possibilities exist.  These Lncl'1de gravity
irrigation below existing small dams, sorie 240 of which were financed by FAC
and FED in the early sixties for water supply. These are rlO longer used for
drinking water, and have been replaced by wells. The average irrigation
potential of each dam is about 40 ha, but only a fraction of the total has
been developed. Other small-scale irrigation possibilities involve pumping
from ponds and all-season rivers, and small perimeters irrigated from shal-
low wells. In addition, many already developed bottomlands could be further
improved by flood protection and other works thus intensifying cultivation.
RDF Il provides for 500 ha to be developed. For costing purposes, it has
been assumed that 300 ha of bottomlands would be improved at an estimated
cost of CFAF 150,000/ha (US$666) and 200 ha of small-scale irrigation devel-
oped at an estimated cost of CFAF 1 million/ha (US$4,400). Cost estimates
are tentative since actual costs would be determined by the physical and
hydrological characteristics of individual sites. In addition to the proj-
ect's small-scale irrigation component, FED is planning a similar program
covering 300 ha (mainly gravity irrigation below existing small dams) over
the next few years.
5.09      HAER would prepare the technical studies for, and construct, the
irrigation works. its staff would be strengthened through expatriate tech-
nical assistance (see para 5.22) to ensure effective execution of project
works. Given the inherent difficulties of this component of RDF II, FDR
would appraise individual proposals for investments in small-scale irri-
gation and improved bottomland development, and compile each year an
annual program of work which would be agreed with IDA before any works
were executed by HAER. Assurances to this effect were obtained during
negotiations.



-13 -
5.10      Wells.  The RDF I well-building program has been executed by four
brigades which would be maintained under RDF II to construct an estimated
520 wells. Costs are estimated at CFAF 58,800 (US$261) per linear meter,
or, with an average well depth of 17.5 meters, at CFAF 1,030,000 (US$4,570)
per well.
5.11      HAER would be responsible for well construction.  Presently all
four well brigades are directly dependent on HAER central maintenance and
administrative facilities in Ouagadougou. This is inefficient; under the
project a temporary base providing storage and maintenance facilities would
be financed. Such a base, consisting of prefabricated buildings and trailers,
would be moved from time to time to keep up with the movements of the four
brigades. The base would be manned by a mechanic and a supply officer, and a
coordinator would be appointed for liaison between HAER headquarters and the
four project brigades; currently the four brigade leaders report directly to
the Director of HAER. At negotiations assurances were obtained that a coor-
dinator with these responsibilities would be appointed before December 31,
1976.
5.12      Tubewells.  Drilling equipment has been financed under the Drought
Relief Project (Credit 442-UV) which is administered by FDR. Drillings with
financing from Credit 442-UV will continue until about June 30, 1977, when
120 wells will have been drilled and funds under Credit 442-UV exhausted.
5.13      Drilling equipment purchased under Credit 442-UV should last at
least five years and drillings after 1977 would be continued with this equip-
ment under RDF Il. On this basis an estimated 180 wells would be drilled
during 1977/78-1979/80 to a depth of 50 meters (average) and 4-1/2" diameter.
Each borehole would be equipped with a manual pump. Costs are estimated at
CFAF 18,600 (US$83) per linear meter or about CFAF 930,000 (US$4,130) per
tubewell. These estimates do not include the costs of the original equip-
ment purchased under Credit 442-UV (amounting to about CFAF 66 mnillion -
about US$300,000).
5.14      Execution of the tubewell drilling program would continue to be
the responsibility of HAER. A tubewell drilling team with a well-equipped
base in Dori is now being formed and trained with technical assistance from
consultants. No change is envisaged for RDF Il tubewell drilling and the
team leader in Dori would remain responsible, as now, to HAER headquarters.
5.15      Village Buildings.  The building of small warehouses under RDF I
was successful. The Fund provided cement, a simple brick press and a mason,
and the village provided free labor. This program will be continued and
an estimated 400 larger warehouses (8 x 3 meters, against 4 x 3 meters in
RDF I), will be constructed. Costs, excluding village labor, are estimated
at CFAF 152,100 (US$676) per unit. Some 20 village centers would also be
constructed on the same basis as the warehouses. These would consist of a
hall (for teaching, meetings, literacy programs, etc.) and a small room
for storage of medical supplies and use by health officials. Estimated



- 14 -
costs are CFAF 258,000 (US$1,150) per center. However, before approving
construction of a village center, FDR would ensure that no equivalent
buildings exist or were planned for the village.
5.16      Agricultural Credit.  In Upper Volta medium-term credit for the
purchase of agricultural equipment is almost nonexistent; most agricul-
tural credit (95%) provided by BND is short-term credit for seasonal inputs
and cotton marketing. USAID recently provided CFAF 63 million (US$280,000),
which is fully disbursed, for medium-term credit for agricultural equipment;
FDR administered the funds.  The terms were 5.5% intereèt with a repay-
ment period of five years, including one year's grace. Under the project
it is proposed that a revolving medium-term credit fund of up to US$0.5
million be created in BND for agricultural equipment and be financed under
the proposed credit. Farmers using the facility, who do not own one drought
animal or agricultural traction equipment, would be required to make a
minimum cash down payment of 10% of the cost of the equipment to be pur-
chased. Credit terms would be identical to the USAID-funded agricultural
credit, except that the interest rate would be raised to 8% to take account
of the recent increase in the rediscount rate from 3.5% to 5.5% granted by
the Central Bank (BCEAO) to BND. The terms of the credit together with other
conditions would be incorporated in the credit agreement to be signed between
the farmer and BND.
5.17      The revolving medium-term credit fund account would be managed
by BND which would appoint, before December 31, 1976, a credit officer to
be responsible full time for its management, and assurances to this effect
were obtained. Applications for medium-term credit would be made through
the ORDs who would appraise the applications with the assistance of a BND
credit officer. The details of these procedures are in Annex 5. The ORD-
appraised applications would be approved by FDR who would be responsible
for authorizing disbursements. This responsibility would rest with FDR
because with its evaluation officers and evaluation procedures at this
time it is better equipped than BND to supervise the use of credit. Sub-
sequently, administration, recovery and relending of the medium-term credit
would be the responsibility of BND or any other agency satisfactory to IDA.
After approval by FDR a credit agreement would be signed between the farmer
and BND. Assurances have been obtained at negotiations that a pro forma
medium-term credit agreement would be prepared by December 31, 1976, and
submitted to IDA for approval.
5.18      Unidentified Projects.  An amount of US$0.6 million would be pro-
vided under the credit to finance other types of subprojects. Proposals
for such subprojects would be submitted to IDA for its agreement to finance
either individual projects or types of projects.
5.19      Maintenance Brigades for Tubewells.  Wells require little main-
tenance, and what is required is done by villagers themselves; tubewells
need to be maintained. Some ORDs have organized a maintenance brigade,
but none exist in the north where project drilLings will be executed.



- 15 -
To maintain tubewells drilled under the project, a small maintenance brigade
would be established, which would be responsible to the team leader of the
tubewell drilling. By the end of the project period, responsibility for
maintenance of project tubewells would be transferred to the benefiting
ORDs, 1/ and assurances to this effect were obtained.
5.20      Supporting Services.  RDF II would provide for the cost of staff,
equipment and operating costs needed by FDR to operate the fund, as well as
direct costs incurred by other participating organizations. In the case
of HAER this would cover the cost of the coordinator of RDF activities,
two topographical brigades, and the four well brigades, and for the ORDs
one topographical brigade, tractors and equipment (mostly for erosion con-
trol works) and extension workers. More details about organization and man-
agement are in Chapter VII.
5.21      Training.  Project institutions would be strengthened by tem-
porary employment of expatriate technical assistance personnel and com-
plemented by training Voltaics to take on project responsibilities. The
project training component would include:
(a)  scholarships (up to 24 individuals would be involved)
for project evaluation officers, engineers, and tech-
nicians, principally for Voltaic counterparts of ex-
patriate staff;
(b)  up to 80 scholarships for short rural development
courses at CESAO 2/ for ORD staff or villagers respon-
sible for small rural development projects; and
(c)  consultancy services for an extension training study
(see para 7.09).
5.22      Technical Assistance.  A number of multilateral and bilateral
agencies have been asked to assist in the execution of the project by pro-
viding expatriate technical assistance. So far the following arrangements
have been concluded:
(a)  Dutch Government:   Provision of three volunteer tech-
nicians to supervise ORD topograph-
ical survey teams;
(b)  FAC:                 Financing of four technicians:  senior
rural engineer, to act as technical
advisor to the RDF coordinator

The three Dutch volunteers arrived in Upper Volta in December 1975; the FAC-

continue to work under RDF II; the FAC-financed senior rural engineer arrived
in January 1976 and the junior rural engineer and the topographical engineer
were recruited in March/April 1976.  A project document for UNDP assistance has
been prepared and submitted to UNDP headquarters which has given permission to
the UNDP Resident Representative in Upper Volta to finance the two evaluation
officers from his technical assistance budget.
5.23      Studies for Economic Development of Areas Freed from Onchocerciasis
(Oncho Studies).  The Volta basin is afflicted by onchocerciasis which brings
iaisery and blindness to people and inhibits development of many fertile val-
leys.  The disease is transmitted by the bite of an infected female blackfly
of the species Simulium damnosum, which carries the disease. An onchocer-
ciasis control program, basically involving the spraying of breeding sites of
the fly with insecticides, is being supported by the international community
and implemented under WHO direction. The program has been in full operation
since December 1974. The effect of control spraying is more or less immedi-
ate although efforts need to be sustained for 20 years to ensure permanent
control. Already, in a few areas, people are returning spontaneously to the
fertile valleys in anticipation of the permanent control of the disease. The
affected valleys constitute one of West Africa's last remaining natural re-
sources and it is imperative that optimal use be made of them, and in par-
ticular, that the traditional destructive methods of land development be
controlled.
5.24      A basic planning methodology for the oncho areas has been developed
by FAO/IBRD missions which visited Upper Volta in 1974 and has been agreed
in principle with Government. Details are in Annex 6. The methodology
involves:
(a) inventory of natural resources in the oncho area
(the data bank);
(b) preparation of a broad development plan for the next
ten years in which development priorities in the oncho
areas are identified (the ten-year development plan); and



- 1 7 -
(c) preparation of specific investment proposals within
the framework of the development plan (project prepa-
ration).
Major surveys and studies needed to complete the data bank are, land use sur-
vey and mapping, associated climatological and hydrological studies; land
suitability survey and mapping; and sociological and economic surveys. The
studies would be executed by consultants working under the direction of the
Ministry of Rural Development in cooperation with AVV and the ORDs. For the
development plan, it is proposed that a small planning cell headed by a senior
advisor (senior oncho areas planning advisor) be established in the Ministry
of Planning. The cell would employ specialized consultants and specialists
on short-term appointments.  For project preparation, provision has also
been made to hire consultancy services to prepare two or three feasibility
studies. The fullest use would be made of local expertise in the oncho work.
Overall responsibility would rest with the Coordinating Committee for Rural
Development, but specific Ministries would be responsible for executing the
various studies (see para 7.11). FDR would be used only as a disbursement
channel. Total costs per consultant employed have been estimated at US$7,500
equivalent per man-month made up as follows: average consultant's fee and
daily allowance US$6,000, international and local travel US$1,000, and US$500
for miscellaneous items. An estimated 75 man-months might be required to
complete this component.
5.25      Feeder Roads.  A road component was included under RDF I which
was jointly executed with similar components financed under Credit 442-UV,
the latter providing funds for equipment. About 350 km of roads have been
successfully improved using labor-intensive methods with active and enthu-
siastic participation by local people. No road component is included in the
proposed project, as Credit 579-UV 1/, a Rural Roads Project, was recently
signed. Under this project, a central Rural Road Unit (SERS - Service En-
tretien des Routes Secondaires) has been created under PWD which will be
responsible for construction, rehabilitation and maintenance of secondary
and feeder roads in the project area and elsewhere. Credit 579-UV will
carry out over the next few years road improvements not financed under RDF
II.  However, all road equipment financed under Credit 317-UV and 442-UV,
and now in the charge of FDR, would be transferred to SERS, upon comple-
tion of the works financed under these credits (estimated in June 1976)
and Government will sign a letter to this effect during credit signing.

6.02      Cost estimates are based on price data obtained during execution
of the RDF I Project, updated to end 1975. However, following recent salary
increases (para 2.04) salaries and wages were updated during negotiations
(April 1976). Project costs are estimated at US$16.2 million or US$15.1
million net of taxes.
6.03      Physical contingencies equivalent to 10% of the costs of project
works and 5% of the costs of supporting services are included in project
costs. Price contingencies have been calculated over base costs and physi-
cal contingencies, and allow for compounded increases based on the follow-
ing annual percentages: for equipment and supplies 10% in 1976, 8% in 1977,
1978 and 1979, and 7% in 1980; and for all other cost items (mainly salaries,
operating costs and labor) 7% annually. No price contingencies have been
used for salaries in 1976, because these were updated to levels paid in 1976
(see para 6.02).

6.05      The proposed IDA credit of US$9.4 million would finance 62% of
project costs net of taxes and would cover the estimated foreign exchange
component (US$7.9 million) and 18% of local costs. The remainder of project
costs would be met by: Banque Arabe pour le Developpement Economique en
Afrique (BADEA); Fonds d'Aide et de Cooperation (FAC); the Dutch Govern-
ment; United Nations Development Program (UNDP); and Government whose share
is equivalent to the estimated taxes.
6.06      The IDA credit would be on standard terms, and the BADEA loan
would be for 25 years with 5 year grace, and with an interest rate to be
set between 2 and 4%. The UNDP, FAC, USAID and the Dutch Government con-
tributions would be grants. Financing arrangements would be parallel.
BADEA's contribution would be for the following sub-component: wells,
tubewells, the maintenance brigade for tubewells and related supporting
services. The contributions of FAC, the Dutch Government and UNDP would



- 20 -
be for expatriate technical assistance. A signed agreement between Govern-
ment and BADEA would be a condition of credit effectiveness.
6.07      All project funds would be passed on to FDR as grants.  FDR would,
as necessary, reallocate them to other participating agencies, HAER and ORDs
as grants. No onlending arrangements are involved as it is proposed that
finance for medium-term agricultural credit would be in a revolving fund
to be managed by BND. During negotiations assurances were obtained that
the revolving fund would be administered by BND under conditions to be de-
termined between BND and Government with IDA agreement.
6.08      As funds available under Credit 317-UV are likely to be exhausted
before funds from the credit made for RDF II become available, it is proposed
that retroactive financing of up to US$350,000 be permitted against expendi-
tures incurred on qualified subprojects 1/ between March 1, 1976 and credit
signature. BADEA would not engage in retroactive financing (see also para
6.11).
Procurement
6.09      Items financed under the IDA credit would be procured as follows:
vehicles, tractors, other equipment and supplies, such as cement and steel,
valued at US$2.0 million, would be obtained through international competi-
tive bidding in accordance with IDA guidelines in the case of contracts
over US$50,000, and through competitive bidding in accordance with local
procedures acceptable to IDA in the case of contracts of less than US$50,000.
For contracts under US$10,000, direct competitive purchasing would be em-
ployed.   Domestically manufactured goods would be allowed a 15% preference
or the applicable import duty, whichever is lower. A large part of project
costs would be for labor, salaries, operating and training expenses, valued
at about US$4.2 million, none of which are suitable for competitive bidding.
The services of consultants and expatriate staff valued at about US$1.4 mil-
lion would be obtained according to procedures acceptable to IDA. A total
amount of US$1.1 million would be for agricultural credit and unidentified
projects. An amount of US$1.8 million would cover contingencies.
6.10      It is recognized that the cofinancing will result in procurement
and disbursement procedures which will vary among the various agencies. For
BADEA's contribution of US$4.5 million, procurement would be in accordance
with BADEA rules. These are not expected to involve Government in higher
costs than if the project were financed only by IDA. The services of ex -
patriate staff and volunteers,valued at about US$1.2 million, financed by
UNDP, FAC, and the Dutch Government, would be obtained in accordance with
these agencies procedures.

Accounts and Audit
6.13      FDR would prepare half yearly budgets which would be submitted to
the Interministerial Technical Committee for approval and to IDA for informa-
tion. On the basis of the approved budgets, Government would make its budget
allocations and would make the necessary funds available to FDR quarterly in
advance. Assurances to this effect were obtained.
6.14      In addition, FDR will prepare and send to IDA biannually in
January and July progress reports and summaries of project expenditures
and use of funds in such detail as IDA may request. Assurances to this
effect were obtained. In addition, a detailed annual report will be pre-
pared, which should be submitted to IDA three months after the end of the
project year.
6.15      FDR would:  (i) keep records adequate to reflect, in accordance
with sound accounting practices, their operations and financial position;
(ii) have their accounts audited by a firm of auditors acceptable to IDA;
(iii) submit copies of their audited accounts and the auditors' report to
IDA within four months of the end of each project year; (iv) see that the
reports of the auditors are of such scope and detail as IDA may reasonably
have requested; and (v) furnish such other information concerning its ac-
counts as IDA may reasonably have required.
6.16      Under RDF I it was required that FDR accounts be audite`d in con-
junction with BND accounts by Caisse Centrale de Cooperation Economique
and the Financial Controller of the Ministry of Finance. This has not
been done; consequently as a condition of credit negotiations FDR appointed
a firm of auditors to audit all FDR accounts. The audit will take place
in May/June 1976 and the auditors' report would be available in the fall
of 1976.
VII. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
7.01      Project implementation, except for the oncho studies, would involve:
the Rural Development Fund (FDR), the Rural Development Works Directorate
(HAER), and the Regional Development Oranizations (ORDs). The responsibili-
ties of these organizations are summarized below. Details are in Annex 9.
7.02      Rural Development Fund (FDR).  FDR will continue, as now, to be
ultimately responsible for project execution; it will appraise subprojects,
assist if necessary in identification and preparation of subprojects, and be
responsible for technical inspection upon completion together with HAER and
for evaluation. FDR staff would be strengthened by two evaluation officers,
an agro-economist and a rural engineer. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that the qualifications, expense, and terms of employment of
the evaluation officers would be satisfactory to IDA. Assurances were also
obtained that as their first task the evaluation officers would evaluate the
program so far financed by FDR in accordance with a schedule to be agreed



- 23 -
with IDA. Following this evaluation and based upon its results an efficient
monitoring system would be designed for evaluation of FDR's further program.
7.03      The FDR accommodation in the BND building is inadequate and other
rented accommodation will be required during the second half of calendar
year 1976. BND levies a 1% service charge on all FDR transactions to cover
rent and other sums that it funds.  This would be adjusted after the FDR
office is moved.
7.04      In addition to subprojects funded under Credit 317-UV, FDR has
administered the Drought Relief Fund Project (Credit 442-UV) and the USAID
medium-term Agricultural Credit Fund (see para 5.16).  In order to ensure
proper management of RDF Il, however, assurances were obtained at negotia-
tions that FDR would not undertake any additional responsibilities without
the agreement of IDA.
7.05      Interministerial Technical Committee.  An interministerial tech-
nical committee was established under Credit 317-UV and a similar intermin-
isterial technical committee was established for Credit 442-UV. The pur-
pose of these committees was to (i) "promote" the goals of the IDA credit
and (ii) provide the necessary coordination and supervision of FDR. The
first function has been taken over to a large extent by the recently estab-
lished Coordinating Committee for Rural Development. The second function
was not executed completely satisfactorily, as membership of the Committee
was too large and few meetings were held. Accordingly, Decree No. 72-142
(Interministerial Technical Committee Credit for 317-UV) and Decree No. 74-
092 (Interministerial Technical Committee for Credit 442-UV) would be abol-
ished and assurances were obtained that a new committee with a membership
satisfactory to IDA would be established not later than September 30, 1976
to supervise FDR. The membership would include the Minister of Rural Devel-
opment (Chairman), the Minister of Planning, the Director of the Permanent
Secretariat of ORDs, the Director of HAER, the Director of Agricultural
Services, the Director of BND, the Director of the Budget, the Director of
the Treasury and the Director of FDR (Secretary). The Directors of the
Departments of Livestock and Forestry would be co-opted if and when FDR
becomes involved in livestock and reafforestation. The Committee would
meet at least twice a year, but preferably quarterly. Since the Director
of the Permanent Secretariat of ORDs is a member, he would decide if there
were any issues which would have to be referred to the Coordinating Commit-
tee for Rural Development.
7.06      Rural Development Works Department (HAER).  Under RDF I, HAER was
responsible for the engineering, design and execution of all subprojects,
but under RDF II HAER would be responsible only for well construction, tube-
well drilling and technically more complex works such as small-scale irriga-
tion and improved bottomland development. 1/ However, HAER would still pro-
vide: (a) guidelines and standards to ORDs for engineering and design; and
1/   If Government established a separate irrigation department responsibil-
ity for the latter works would, after IDA agreement, be transferred to
this new department.



- 24 -
(b) technical inspection upon completion ("reception") of all projects to-
gether with FDR. Assurances reflecting the changes in HAER's responsibili-
ties were obtained at negotiations.
7.07      Staffing has always been a limiting factor in HAER's executive
capacity. Under the project an assistant to the Director would be appointed
to coordinate all FDR activities being handled by HAER.  This appointment
would be a condition of credit effectiveness. FAC is providing technical
assistance to HAER through four expatriate experts (see para 5.22) and this
should meet HAER's needs for technical personnel.
7.08      Regional Development Organization.  The role of ORDs under the
second project .will be expanded. In addition to identifying subprojects,
providing extension, credit and input supply services to participants on
productive subprojects and supervising the maintenance of completed subproj-
ects, they will also undertake engineering, design and execution of simple
works such as bottomlands, erosion control works and village buildings.
ORDs would be strengthened to undertake these tasks and funds would be
provided under RDF II for additional topographical survey teams and trac-
tors and equipment needed for land development. Lack of funding has re-
sulted in shortages in ORD staffing in the past; therefore funding of ex-
tension workers to supervise maintenance of and provide extension services
for farmers participating in productive subprojects, started under RDF I,
will be continued and expanded under RDF II. The ORDs would be responsible
for supervising the management and maintenance by farmers of subprojects
once construction is completed and since they also provide the necessary
services, ORDs would therefore be the critical element in ensuring the suc-
cess of the various subprojects. At negotiations.assurances were obtained
that Government would provide adequate funds and support to ORDs to enable
them to provide the necessary services to ensure successful and efficient
operation and maintenance of subprojects.
7.09      Training.  A small training component, mainly scholarship, has
been included in the project. Details are in Annex 10. However, although
current output of extension workers in Upper Volta may be sufficient, there
are some doubts about the quality of extension worker training and adequacy
of training facilities in relation to future manpower requirements. Govern-
ment is aware of this and has certain ideas on possible solutions. What is
needed however is a short study to pull the various elements and ideas to-
gether and provide a concise proposal for future action. An extension
training study would be completed by June 1977, and submitted to IDA for
information. Assurances to this effect were obtained together with assur-
ances that the terms of reference, terms of employment and qualifications
of consultants who might be hired under the training component of the
project would be satisfactory to IDA. The Ministry of Rural Development
would in principle be responsible for executing this study, but for prac-
tical purposes the small disbursement involved would be channeled through
FDR.



- 25 -
