7.11      Oncho Studies.  Overall responsibility for the oncho studies would
rest with the existing Coordinating Committee for Rural Development and as-
surances were obtained that this Committee would be maintained. Immediate
responsibility for the data bank and feasibility studies will rest with the
Ministry of Rural Development and the Ministry of Planning will be responsi-
ble for the ten-year development plan. For that purpose, the Ministry of
Planning will be strengthened by the appointment of a Senior Adviser who
will be internationally recruited with qualifications and terms of appoint-
ment satisfactory to IDA. Assurances to this effect were obtained. Consul-
tants are expected to carry out most of the studies and assurances were
obtained that consultants would have terms of reference, terms of employ-
ment and qualifications satisfactory to IDA.
7.12      AVV is responsible for the economic development of well-defined
areas along the Volta Rivers. Recently consultants, financed by UNDP with
the Bank as executing agency, reported on AVV's organization, its future
role and related organizational and financial requirements. This report
is presently being studied by Government and its implications being dis-
cussed with IDA. During negotiations assurances were obtained that Govern-
ment would present to IDA not later than March 31, 1977 its proposals with
respect to detailed tasks and specific responsibilities of its agencies
which will execute development projects in the areas freed from onchocer-
ciasis.
VIII. FARMERS' BENEFITS AND FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS FOR GOVERNMENT
Farmers Benefits from Productive Subprojects
8.01      Farmers would obtain direct benefits in the form of salable or con-
sumable farm products from the bottomland, small-scale irrigation, erosion
control, and medium-term credit subprojects. The principal products would
be rice, sorghum and millet; there would be lesser quantities of groundnuts,
cotton and vegetables.
8.02      If the mix of subprojects envisaged in Chapter V is implemented
and if on average they reflect the "model" subprojects described in Annexes
4 and 11, it is estimated that by 1980 RDF Il would be generating 5,300 tons
of paddy (3,400 tons of rice equivalent), 1,200 tons of cereals and some 200
tons of other products annually with a total financial farm gate value of
CFAF 217 million (US$965,000). These returns are based on official farmgate



- 27 -
producer prices prevailing in late 1975: paddy CFAF 35/kg; sorghum/millet
CFAF 22/kg; groundnuts CFAF 25/kg and seed cotton CFAF 39/kg. These prices
are also used in the farm budget analyses described below. Their use in the
analyses is conservative since prices paid by private traders, who dominate
the marketing chain in Upper Volta, are generally higher.
8.03      It is estimated that as many as 15,000 farm families (between
120,000-190,000 people) could benefit from one or more of the directly pro-
ductive RDF II subprojects if the tentative program in Chapter V is imple-
mented. More families would benefit from bottomland development than any
other type of project since it is usual to allot about 0.2 ha per family
in such schemes, consequently, 13,500 families could participate in these
subprojects; another 1,000 in small-scale irrigation; and about 2,500 in
erosion control schemes. About 1,700 families are estimated to make use
of the medium-term credit facility. All of these people would be among
the poorest in Upper Volta. In addition, many more people will benefit
from the wells, warehouses and village centers constructed under the proj-
ect, which would affect an estimated 600 villages.
8.04      Models of typical holdings together with farm budget analyses are
given in Annex 11, and summarized below:
8.05      Bottomlands.  Assuming 0.2 ha per participating farm family, a
yield of 1.5 tons paddy/ha, with no inputs other than improved seed, family
income 1/ is estimated at CFAF 9,800 (US$45) annually and the return per
man-day employed in cultivating rice at about CFAF 445 (US$2). In the case
of improved bottomlands, yields would amount to 2.2 tons; incremental family
income over traditional bottomland income would be CFAF 4,900 (US$22); 2/
and the return per man-day would rise from CFAF 445 to CFAF 668 (US$3) fol-
lowing improvement.
8.06      Small-scale irrigation.  Benefits from small irrigation schemes
would vary widely depending on the cropping patterns adopted and the amount
of double cropping. Where paddy is grown, average yields of 3,500 kg/ha/crop
together with a cropping intensity of 150% could be expected. Assuming a plot
of 0.2 ha a family growing rice on such a scheme would receive, after the
purchase of inputs, an income 3/ estimated at CFAF 27,000 (US$120) and a
return per man-day employed of CFAF 697 (US$3.10).
8.07      Erosion control works.  The erosion control works would improve the
retention and percolation of rain water and slow down the degradation of soil
1/   Bottomlands are generally not farmed before development, thus family
income from this source would be additional to income from traditional
holdings.
2/   Family income for a 0.2 ha holding would increase from CFAF 9,800 for
a bottomland to CFAF 14,700 following improvement.
3/   Income from an 0.2 irrigated holding would be additional to income
from a traditional holding.



- 28 -
structure. The direct impact of these improvements on yields is impossible
to determine and on an annual basis would be very small. However, partici-
pants in these subprojects would also receive and employ a simple package of
improved technology (seed of improved varieties, seed dressing, fertilizer
and pesticide sprays for cotton and advice on better spacing and timing of
cultivation). These improvements together with the soil erosion control
measures are expected to result in increments in yield of 150 kg/ha for
sorghum, 135 kg/ha for unshelled groundnuts, and 200 kg for seed cotton.
On a typical Mossi farm of 4 ha located in an erosion control perimeter
incremental net income (after payment for inputs) would be an estimated CFAF
13,000 (US$60) 1/ and the return per man-day employed would increase by 30%
from CFAF 157 to CFAF 204.
8.08      Medium-term credit.  The credit would be for animal drawn farming
equipment. The impact of the use of this equipment would be to save labor,
thus permitting, first, the more timely cultivation of existing land (bottle-
necks occur at the beginning of the planting season) and second, the cultiva-
tion of more land if the family has such a facility. In the model at Annex 11,
it is assumed that the family would cultivate an additional 2 ha and net incre-
mental family income on such basis is estimated at about CFAF 40,000 (US$175).
Financial Implications for Government
8.09      Government's contribution to project investment costs would be
equivalent to the estimated tax component of the project during the proj-
ect period. Thereafter, Government would have to meet the costs of exten-
sion and other farmer support services provided to participants on the di-
rectly productive subprojects. Directly related project costs are estimated
at about CFAF 30-40 million (US$130,000-180,000) annually. Subsequent to 1986
Government would have to meet repayments of the IDA credit amounting to an
average of US$240,000 annually and of the BADEA loan amounting to an average
US$300,000 annually. Project participants would not pay back the investment
costs of subprojects, since investments are generally small, participants are
poor, and natural conditions on the Mossi plateau are harsh. However mainte-
nance costs (mainly labor) would be borne by participants. Government would
thus not receive any direct offsetting revenues other than stabilization fund
revenues if any on cotton and groundnuts and indirect taxes generated by par-
ticipants' greater purchasing power. Indirectly project activities will re-
quire Government expenditures for rural sector overheads, such as in ORDs,
HAER and its Ministry of Rural Development.  Government is aware that expend-
itures for Rural Development will have to be increased and this will be stud-
ied and discussed from time to time during supervision of the proposed project.
IX. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
9.01      The project provides a line of credit to FDR for investment in
small-scale scattered subprojects, selected in accordance with procedures
1/ Net value of production would rise from CFAF 43,000 (US$190) to CFAF
56,000 (US$250).



- 29 -
set forth in Chapter VII and detailed in Annex 9. Although benefits for
most individual subprojects can be identified (see farmer benefits on
typical holdings in Chapter VIII) some will provide social and health
benefits which are less easily quantified.
9.02      The principal economic and quantifiable benefits of the project
would be as follows: Bottomlands - increases in wet season rice production;
small-scale irrigation - increases in rice and season vegetable production;
erosion control and medium-term credit - increased production of cereals and
groundnuts; warehouses - reduction of storage losses in both farm inputs
and farm outputs, plus induced increases in input use; and wells and tube-
wells - improved labor productivity and increased livestock production.
9.03      The project will also provide important social and institutional
benefits. Wells give an assured water supply to the human population, who
otherwise depend on small often distant creeks which run dry for at least
three months of the year. There is also a significant health impact as the
creeks contain waterborne diseases. Institutional strengthening has already
occurred under the first project by improving the capacity of FDR, HAER and
ORDs to sponsor and implement sound development plans. The proposed strength-
ening of these institutions under the project (see Chapter VII) would provide
further improvements.
9.04      To calculate project costs, a development program for works to
be executed has been determined (see Chapter V). Neither the program, nor
the annual pace of development can be determined in advance, but on the
basis of the assumptions and the various benefits described in Chapter
VIII, the estimated economic rate of return from the Rural Development
Fund investment package is 16%. Excluded from the calculation is the cost
of unidentified projects, and studies for economic development of areas
freed from onchocerciasis. The onchocerciasis eradication program is eco-
nomically justified (see Annex 6).
9.05      The economic rate of return calculations are tentative as the
project provides basically a line of credit to FDR. Therefore separate
rates of return for various subcomponents are hardly meaningful and are
very arbitrary as it is impossible to accurately allocate overheads and to
determine the annual pace of development.  However, in order to obtain some
idea about the ranking of the various subcomponents, based on simple models
and the development program in Chapter V, assuming that benefits described
in Chapter VIII will materialize and excluding overheads, the estimated
subproject economic rates of return are: bottomlands, over 50%, erosion
control 30%, improved bottomlands 18%, small-scale irrigation 16%, and agri-
cultural equipment, over 50%. The rate of return for wells and tubewells
is 6% (for details see Annex 12) but the benefits do not include improve-
ments in health and guaranteed water supplies. If wells and tubewells are
excluded the overall economic rate of return would be 21%.
9.06      The above rates of return do not take into account two important
aspects: (i) that the main benefits accrue to the poorest segment of the
population in an area with difficult ecological and human conditions, and
(ii) the impact on human well-being, such as subprojects providing improved



- 30 -
village water supplies. The rate of return is lower than for other agri-
cultural projects financed by IDA in Upper Volta, but this results directly
from the choice of the central plateau as the area of concentration of
RDF II. Under the very difficult ecological and human conditions of the
plateau, rates in excess of 10% must be considered quite satisfactory.
9.07      Risks.  As with all agricultural operations in Upper Volta, ben-
efits are liable to sharp fluctuations due to notoriously variable rain-
fall. Other major risks are that it may be difficult to ensure (a) ade-
quate supervision and support for subprojects scattered over a large area;
and (b) that local communities obtain benefits from individual subprojects.
Under the project the organizations would be strengthened which should mini-
mize risks.
X. AGREEMENTS REACHED
10.01     The following principal agreements have been reached with the Bor-
rower during negotiations:
(a) except for well and tubewell constructions, (i) no more
than 10% of project funds would be spent on works outside
the five Mossi plateau ORDs, and (ii) subprojects in the
valleys of the Voltas would be excluded (AR 1/ para 4.01
and CA 2/ 3.06);
(b) The annual program for small-scale irrigation and improved
bottomland development will be appraised by FDR and agreed
with IDA, before any works are executed by HAER (AR para
5.09 and CA 3.07);
(c) A coordinator responsible for liaison between the four
project well brigades, and HAER would be appointed
before December 31, 1976 (AR para 5.11 and CA 3.05);
(d) BND would appoint before December 31, 1976 a credit officer
who would be responsible for management of the project medi-
um-term credit fund (AR para 5.17 and CA 3.09);
(e) A proforma medium-term credit agreement would be prepared
by December 31, 1976 and submitted to IDA for approval (AR
para 5.17 and CA 3.09);

(estimated in June 1976) and Government will sign a letter to that effect
during credit signing (AR para 5.25).

would be suitable for an IDA credit of US$9.4 million.

ANNEX 2
Page 1
UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
First Rural Development Fund Project
A. Original Objectives
1,        The First Rural Development Fund Project (RDF I) was appraised in
August 1971; the credit was signed in June 1972 (Credit 317-UV) and became
effective in October 1972. The Project's objective as defined at appraisal 1/
was to set up a Rural Development Fund to help finance a large number of
small-scale rural development activities designed to increase agricultural
production and to improve the health and well being of rural communities.
The small-scale activities consisted of a program of construction within a
three-year period of about:
(a)  500 wells for domestic water supplies;
(b)  700 village warehouses for storing farm inputs;
(c)  190 small land and water development projects, which
consisted of water control and swampy depressions,
irrigation works below existing dams and soil erosion
control. More detailed estimates were:
(i)  1,500 of bas fonds development (150 projects with
an average of 10 ha);
(ii) 200 ha small irrigation (10 projects with an average
of 20 ha); and
(iii)  1,000 ha soil erosion control works (20 projects of
50 ha each);
(d) improvement of about 270 km of feeder roads; and
(e) a number of unidentified projects.
Project costs were estimated at US$3.04 million. An IDA credit of US$2.2
million was made to cover 72% of project costs. French bilateral assistance
in the form of seconded expatriate staff amounted to US$0.38 million or 13%
of project costs and Governnent budgetary allocations axnounted to US$0.46
1/   See Report PA-127a, Appraisal of a Rural Development Fund Project Upper
Volta, dated May 16, 1972.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
million or 15% of project costs; Government contribution was offset by taxes
and duties which were estimated at appraisal to amount to about 15% of proj-
ect costs.
2.        The idea of a Rural Development Fund originated as a result of the
Bank's first economic mission to Upper Volta in November 1969 1/, and stemmed
from the mission's conclusions that:
(a) there was a perceived need by local communities for
small-scale development actions and that if these
could be initiated they would be supported by the
local population;
(b) in the past a number of projects in the rural sector
had failed because of lack of local support;
(c) foreign aid sources, which provide most of the money
for development finance in Upper Volta, had up to then
shown little interest in financing such small-scale
actions;
(d) as Government had started to decentralize its services
with the creation of Regional Development Organizations
(ORDs) agencies now existed that were capable of identi-
fying and implementing such small projects;
(e) ORD financing, however, was coming mainly from aid
sources and was tied to specific programs that did
not include this type of project; and thus
(f) some form of mechanism to finance such actions was re-
quired.
An IDA appraisal mission pursued the matter further, and Credit 317-UV was
approved to finance a Rural Development Fund (RDF I) that itself constituted
a line of credit to finance small-scale development actions. The basic
arrangement was that the Fund would appraise proposals made to it by the ORD
as and when suitable small-scale projects were identified and prepared rather
that the more usual system of following a clearly defined program of actions
and technical and economic viability of which is determined in advance.
3.        Responsibility for execution of RDF I was shared between three
institutions. FDR was created as an operating division of the National
Development Bank (BND) to manage the Fund administratively and financially,
and to evaluate subprojects: the Rural Engineering Division of the Ministry
of Agriculture (which later became the Rural Development Works Department
1/   See Report AW-19a, The Economic Development of Upper Volta, dated
November 17, 1974, in four volumes.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
(HAER) under the Ministry of Rural Development) was responsible for wells,
tubewells, and land and water development, and the Ministry of Public Works
(PWD) for feeder roads. To help them undertake these tasks, the Rural
Engineering Division and PWD were reinforced with additional personnel,
totaling three Voltaic engineers and six expatriates; the latter were to
be provided and financed by French technical assistance (FAC). Government
agreed to supply additional extension personnel to the ORDs to supervise
FDR funded projects as a condition of the credit 1/.
B. Summary of Physical Achievements
4.        Although the project became operational in October 1972, it had a
slow start for several reasons:
(a)  implementation of a program of subprojects for the first
dry season (1972/73) was delayed by the late recruitment
of key personnel for HAER and PWD;
(b) financial difficulties arose at the start of the project
because Government failed to prefinance project expendi-
tures (prefinancing was agreed only in April 1973); and
(c) delays occurred in the acquisition of equipment (both in
bidding procedures and in equipment deliveries to Upper
Volta) which affected in particular the well construction
program.
5.        After these initial difficulties were overcome, project implemen-
tation accelerated rapidly. Physical achievements as of June 30, 1975, are
summarized below and detailed by ORD in Table 1.
1/   RDF I appraisal report para 5.11 and Credit Agreement 317-UV
(para 3.08(a)).



ANNEX 2
Page 4

/1   As of June 30, 1975.
/2   A few boreholes have also been drilled experimentally.
/3   Irrigated horticulture perimeters are included.
By June 30, 1976, when project funds are fully exhausted, it is estimated
that the following additional works will have been completed: 100 ha bottom-
lands, 200 ha erosion control works, and 50 village warehouses.
6.        Farmer Support.  An important criteria for the selection of sub-
projects under RDF I was that they were perceived as a need by local people
who were prepared to participate, by providing free labor, in their construc-
tion. After an initial hesitant and a wait and see attitude, in the early
stage of RDF I farmer response became increasingly favorable as the project
gained momentum and became more widely known in rural areas and manual labor
required for construction of project activities has been voluntarily pro-
vided by village communities. But for road construction labor, villagers
received small bonuses. Given the prevailing local support, Rural Develop-
ment Fund Operations can continue. Farmers are also required, after com-
pletion of construction, to provide labor for maintenance. Yet experience
to date is too short to make either a negative or positive assessment of
farmer response in this respect. Works seem generally well maintained,
but lack of sufficient ORD support in certain cases resulted in sub-optimal
maintenance.
7.        Erosion Control Works have not been as extensively executed as
planned. They were concentrated in the ORD of Ouagadougou which had already
acquired experience with these works under other programs in earlier years.
Farmers' support is strong and works have been executed as expected. Yields
have also increased, but sufficient data have not yet been collected to com-
pare results with appraisal estimates.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
8.        Irrigation below Dams.  This subcomponent has not been successful,
basically from lack of qualified personnel. Works that were carried out,
showed shortcomings in design and low quality in construction because of
insufficient supervision. Under the project 29 ha of irrigation below dams
and 39 ha of irrigated horticulture perimeters were developed. The 29 ha
perimeter was inundated and destroyed by floods during the 1975 rainy season.
The horticulture perimeters are lands irrigated from a well; they were mostly
developed near Bobo-Dioulasso and are successfully maintained and used. Al-
though experience is limited, it seems that these irrigated horticulture peri-
meters are rather successful. Because of experience under RDF I, procedures
for implementation of small irrigation works (irrigation below dams, irriga-
tion by pumping from lakes, or irrigation from wells) need to be tightened.
Although partly a failure, it was also partly a success and therefore
continued allowance for development of small irrigation has been made under
RDF II.
9.        Village Warehouses have been generally well constructed.  It was
originally envisaged that these facilities would be used for the storage of
farm inputs, such as seeds, fertilizer and pesticides. It was assumed that
cotton growers would be the principal beneficiaries from the storage facili-
ties, because of increased yields due to better germination of seed if it is
properly stored. In addition there would be reduced losses of fertilizer
and other inputs through better storage. However, because of the drought,
cotton production has sharply declined on the Mossi plateau. Instead of being
used for input storage, village warehouses have been in strong demand by ORDs
for storing foodgrain bought by them from the farmers, since storage losses
are thereby reduced.
10.       Bottomland Development.  About 1,000 ha of bottomlands have been
developed under RDF I. Works consist of the establishment of small dikes
in bottomlands following contour lines, which in principle, enable water
retention both over a larger area and for a longer period, thus increasing
the area available for paddy cultivation and making more water available
during the crucial flowing period in September, thus increasing yields.
11.       Works have been organized as follows:  (a) extension agents from
ORDs have organized village meetings, to obtain farmers' consent to develop
the bottomlands, their cooperation in carrying out the establishment of
contour dikes and their agreement to distribute plots according to work
performed by individuals; (b) surveyors from HAER carried out a topographical
survey, established field pickets following contour lines and dew contour
maps; (c) tractors equipped with disc-plots, owned or hired by ORDs, have
lossened the soil along contour lines, and sometimes plowed the entire area
to be cultivated if the land was sufficiently levelled; mechanical land
levelling has only been carried out on rare occasions; and (d) local farmers
have established and compacted contour dikes (under HAER supervision), and
where necessary the services of qualified masons have been used to complete
control structures such as culverts and small spillways.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
12.       The establishment of contour dikes has, on the whole, given the
results that could be expected from it. Rice cultivated area has increased
and yields on developed bottomlands have on average been 500 kg to 600 kg
higher than on undeveloped bottomlands. In 1974/75, yield data were col-
lected for the first time by FDR. These data revealed that yields varied
from 0.9 tons of paddy/ha to 3.5 tons/ha, and averaged about 1.5 tons/ha;
rice yields in underdeveloped bottomlands were tentatively estimated at 0.9
tons/ha. Most bottomlands (80%) developed under RDF I are located on the
Mossi plateau. Farmer response has been satisfactory but extension follow-
up could have been better.
13.       Results have been satisfactory but different from those estimated
at appraisal. It was initially expected at appraisal that bottomland devel-
opment would be more intensive than the relatively simple contour dike system,
and would involve small weirs with lateral irrigation ditches and drains that
would permit a high degree of water control. Average investment costs were
calculated at 74,000 CFAF per ha, and yields were estimated to reach about 3
tons of paddy/ha. These more sophisticated structures have not been built,
mainly for two reasons:
(a) hydrological data on rainfall intensity in the watershed
and flood behavior in bottomlands, necessary for the
design of such structures, have not been collected; 1/
(b) neither ORDs nor HAER have sufficiently qualified person-
nel to carry out the engineering, design, and execution
of such more sophisticated works.
14.       The more traditional type of bottomland develoment used in RDF I
has led to cost savings: actual costs during the 1974/75 season did not
exceed 40,000 CFAF per ha. Conversely, average yields on such bottomlands
are estimated at 1.5 tons of paddy per ha. The more traditional method of
bottomland development will be continued under RDF II (see Annex 4), although
some provision has been made for a small program of more sophisticated
bottomland development, more-or-less the type originally envisaged for RDF I.
15.       Well Construction.  It was envisaged that 500 wells would be con-
structed under RDF I (120 in 1972/73; 100 in 1973/74 and 220 in 1974/75).
Three well construction brigades were to be responsible for subproject execu-
tion. It was envisaged that voluntary village labor would be used for digging
and lining the wells under the supervision of paid masons. Each well was to
be concrete-lined and have a concrete surround; no head works were to be pro-
vided as water would be drawn by simple rope and container. Expected costs
were estimated at CFAF 400,000 per well, or CFAF 22,500 (US$112.50) per linear
meter.
1/   Data on the size, vegetation and rainfall in specific watersheds
exist only in a study of some 100 bottomlands around Ouagadougou.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
16.       It appears that well construction costs were underestimated at the
time of appraisal. In 1971/72, wells built on force account in Niger were
already costing CFAF 30,000 per linear meter, and wells built by private
enterprise in the People's Republic of Benin cost CFAF 50,000. The main
cause of the underestimation was an overoptimistic evaluation of the potential
operating efficiency of the brigades rather than an underestimation of unit
costs. Average rate of construction was estimated at 55 wells per brigade
per year (500 wells by three teans over three years), whereas the actual rate
of construction was 20 in 1972/73, and rose to 40 in the 1974/75 season.
17.       The formation of the brigades was slow and delayed the construction
program. Four brigades were ultimately financed under RDF I and the same
number will be financed under RDF II. Delays in the arrival of compressors,
pumps and drilling equipment prevented the brigades from digging deep enough,
particularly in rocky layers. A result of this is that the majority of
wells are too shallow and run dry at the end of the dry season. Progress
of construction is presented in the following table:
Number of wells   Remaining

18.       Three of the well brigades follow the work method described in
paragraph 15. A different method has been introduced by one of the more re-
cently established brigades, i.e., construction of wells in one continuous
grab bucket operation. This method is more rapid, requires somewhat more
equipment but needs less salaried manpower and less voluntary labor. The
latter is also important as experience shows that village labor is willing
to participate, but generally is not available before the end of harvesting
which occurs well into the dry season (December/January). This new method
is, however, somewhat more complex than the traditional one, and needs
experience and continuous supervision.
19.       Currently well brigades lack sufficient core drills and jackham-
mers, and the derricks have insufficient power. Explosives are not used
but their use is strongly recommended in order to speed up work in rock
layers. Needed equipment and materials, together with required replace-
ments, are detailed in Annex 4, and would be provided under the project.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
20.       The professionals in charge of the well brigades (three Voltaics
and one expatriate) are well qualified; the Voltaics have progressively
acquired experience and are now able to complete wells that are entirely
satisfactory. To increase operating efficiency, however, it would be
necessary:
(a) to establish annual well digging programs in such a way
that the teams can work in close proximity, thus facil-
itating the supply of materials and supervision of works;
and
(b) to make one of the team heads responsible for supervising
the total RDF II well construction program and all field
teams. One of the Voltaic brigade leaders would be well
qualified for this role.
21.       Road Development.  Road development has been executed under
Credits 317-UV and 442-UV jointly. Equipment purchases were financed under
Credit 442-UV, the remainder of the costs was met from 317-UV. This partly
explains why a greater length of roads has been constructed than estimated
at appraisal. The different roads developed and their estimated costs are
summarized in the table below:

22.       Road construction has been executed using labor intensive methods,
and villagers have been very willing to participate in road construction
work. To a large extent the success of this subcomponent has been due to
the energetic supervision of the road engineer provided by FAC. The road
component will not be continued under RDF II, since rural road development
is now financed under the IDA-funded Rural Roads Project (Credit 579-UV).
23.       Unidentified Subprojects.  Subprojects executed under the un-
identified components include:
(i)  eight large-size warehouses for use by ORDs (sizes
2       2            2
50 m , 250 m , and 300 m
(ii) eleven livestock pents for smallholders to facilitate
production of farm yard manure;
(iii) a cold storage warehouse (FDR financed part of this
investment; the ORD of Bobo-Dioulasso financed the
greatest part; the warehouse is to be used for
storage of vegetables); and
(iv) feasibility study of irrigation in the Kamadena plain.
The study was awarded to BDPA-GERSAR in August 1974,
and is now being finalized.
C. Performance of Participating Agencies
24.       FDR (Fonds de Developpement Rural).  Since few subprojects were
proposed by ORDs in the project's early stages, the project manager's main
task initially was one of helping ORDs to identify subprojects, rather than
appraising and supervising projects generated by ORDs, as was thought at
appraisal would be the case. The general success of RDF I is a tribute to
the project manager's efforts to identify and establish a strong and co-
operative relationship between ORDs and FDR. The post of evaluation officer
in FDR has, however, never been filled and this is a serious deficiency.
In addition the accounts of FDR have yet to be audited and a detailed
analytical accounting system is needed, as is an improved system of stock
valuation and control.
25.       Under RDF Il, FDR management will be strengthened through provision
of additional staff, including two expatriate evaluation officers. The
audit of FDR accounts is now being organized.



ANNEX 2
Page 10
26.       The Rural Water and Land Development Division (HRAT) 1/  Within
the Rural Development Water Department (HAER) was charged with the technical
preparation and execution of all subprojects except feeder roads. Although
HRAT has made commendable efforts to carry out its tasks, it has continuously
suffered from a high turnover of personnel and as a consequence was seriously
understaffed most of the time. The situation as of April 1975 serves as an
illustration of the difficult situation.
HRAT key personnel working           Positions agreed at            April 1975

The vacant positions are now being filled by technical assistance personnel
provided by FAC.
27.       In addition to lack of staff, persistent problems have arisen in
personnel allocation. RDF I-financed personnel is integrated into the HAER
organization, but since it is responsible to the Director of HAER and not
FDR, this has led to such personnel being allocated at times to non-FDR-
financed projects. This occurred much more following the departure of the
senior rural engineer, whose task was, amongst others, to coordinate all
work of HAER personnel where FDR activities were concerned.  However, it
should also be noted that although HAER needed technical assistance in order
to be capable of executing project works, these jobs did not at all times
provide full employment.  Therefore, as long as FDR financed projects are
properly prepared and executed, there should be no objection against using
technical assistance to the fullest extent possible. An alternative method is
where all project works are executed by a project management unit, but this is
against the basic approach of the Rural Development Fund Project, which is to
be a source of finance for projects executed by other institutions. Whilst
there is always the danger that staff and other resources financed by FDR
1/   At the time of appraisal of RDF I, the Rural Development Works Department
(HAER) was called Hydraulique et Equipement Rural and the Rural Water
and Land Development Division (HRAT) was called Hydraulique et Amenagement
de l'Espace Rural.



ANNEX 2
Page 11
can be diverted to other purposes because they are not directly controlled
by FDR.  This issue is not seen as a major problem, since FDR exercises a
financial controlling device. Furthermore, where such resources would
otherwise be underemployed, then additional alternative usage should be
encouraged.
28.       Under RDF I, HAER was responsible for preparation and execution
of all project works, except roads. This arrangement will be changed under
RDF II, since ORDs will be responsible for the bottomland and anti-erosion
works development. HAER as the technical department will however have to
safeguard technical standards by regular supervision. It will also be
responsible for the small-scale irrigation component and the well and tube-
well subcomponents of the project.
29.       The Public Works Department (PWD) had to delay the start of its
operations due to the late arrival of the road engineer (in November 1973).
Little road construction equipment was purchased under the Credit 317-UV
following a decision to hire existing PWD equipment. This has proved to
be costly and has resulted in the start of numerous but small and scattered
road improvements. The situation has greatly improved since it was decided
to buy road construction equipment under the Drought Relief Project (442-UV)
and to establish an independent feeder road unit, headed by the RDF I road
engineer. The road unit has performed remarkably well under the able
guidance of the expatriate road engineer. Labor intensive construction
methods have been employed which have been appreciated by the villagers
involved in road construction.
30.       Since rural roads in general are now going to be improved under
the responsibility of SERS (Service Entretien des Routes Secondaires - a
new service created under PWD as part of the Bank's Rural Road Project),
the road component will not be continued under RDF II. Equipment purchased
under Credits 317-UV and 442-UV will be handed over to SERS.
31.       The Regional Development Offices (ORDs) have, as a whole, made
a strong effort to use available RDF funds effectively. A major problem
for most ORDs, however, is their inability to engage additional personnel
needed for the supervision and provision of support services for subprojects.
Government has failed to increase ORD budgets to the extent necessary to
make adequate allowances for these services, in spite of an obligation to
do so under section 3.08 of the Credit Agreement.
32.       The dangers of inadequate supervision and failure to provide sup-
port services to participants in subprojects was pointed out in a bottom-
land development project carried out with French bilateral aid funds from
1969 to 1972. It is estimated that some 35% of all bottomlands developed
were abandoned, mostly because of the failure of the implementing agency
to (i) correct design deficiencies and ensure adequate maintenance; and
(ii) to provide participants with the advice and inputs needed to make
best use of the developments. Because of this, IDA agreed at the end of



ANNEX 2
Page 12
1974 that FDR could provide financing to ORDs for the employment of ad-
ditional extension personnel. Some 40 extension workers have since been
recruited and placed in different ORDs.
33.       An Interministerial Technical Committee was created at the
start of RDF I to examine and approve all projects proposed for financing
hy FDR. The Committee consists of about 15 members, representing the
different Government ministries and agencies concerned (Agriculture, Plan-
ning, Public Works, Finance, ORDs). Meetings were to be convened at the
initiative of the Director of FDR. In practice, the Committee has been
convened on average only once per year, as it proved cumbersome to
organize such meetings. The Committee has, however, provided a useful
forum to "publicize" FDR intentions and support project management.
34.       Now that Coordinating Committee for Rural Development has
been established, this body would provide the form for "publicizing" all
rural development activities, and therefore this function should no longer
be performed by the Interministerial Technical Committee. Membership of
this Committee could therefore be reduced (see Annex 9) so as to include
only those directly related with project execution. This would make the
Committee more effective.
D. Costs
35.       The cashflow of the First Rural Development Fund Project is in
Table 2; a statement of receipts and expenditures is in Table 3; and a
summary of disbursements under Credit 317-UV is in Table 4.
36.       As of June 30, 1975, an amount of CFAF 441.4 million had been
spent. Reimbursements by IDA until that date amounted to US$1,683,000,
leaving a balance of about US$517,000 (or CFAF 116 million at an ex-
change rate of US$1.00 = CFAF 225). This is insufficient to cover expendi-
tures of a 1975/76 campaign of the size considered by FDR to be necessary
and realistic. Consequently it is proposed that retroactive financing be
provided to finance some FDR activities in 1975/76.
37.       The IDA credit of US$2.2 million was expected to cover expenditures
of CFAF 562.6 million, and an exchange rate of US$1.00 = CFAF 255.8 was used
at appraisal. In practice, however, the average exchange rate for disburse-
ments was US$1.00 = CFAF 220.55, and the credit was equivalent to only
CFAF 485 million, or 86% of the original estimate. The project also suffered
from the steep world-wide inflation that began in 1973. Provision for price
inflation was only 5%. With hindsight, it is clear that this was grossly
insufficient; contingencies amounting to 20-30% of project costs would have
been more appropriate.



ANNEX 2
Page 13
38.       A comparison of unit costs, appraisal estimate and actual, for
some of the subprojects is given below:

E. Undertakings under Credit 317-UV
39.       Section 3.02 of the Credit Agreement deals with the project manage-
ment unit. Under this section the Borrower agreed to establish a Rural
Development Division with BND, to cause BND to employ a project manager,
deputy project manager and an evaluation officer, to cause BND to keep a
consolidated account and separate accounts for each ORD by class of projects,
and to cause HAER to employ a sufficient number of qualified engineers.
Accounts are discussed below in para 45. The project has been managed accord-
ing to organization envisaged at appraisal. However, staffing has been a
problem throughout the project period and this will have to be improved
under RDF II especially with regard to project evaluation.
40.       Under Section 3.03 the Borrower agreed to advance funds quarterly
for the operations of FDR. As noted above one of the reasons for a slow
start was that Government was not prepared to advance funds until April 1973
(see para 4). However, since that date, no further problems have been
encountered.
41.       Under Section 3.04 it was envisaged that ORDs would prepare projects
and under section 3.05 that FDR would appraise such projects. As noted above
FDR has given considerable assistance to ORDs in the preparation of projects.
Although ORDs have now gained experience in dealing with FDR, a lot depends
on their actual staffing and the continued assistance of FDR in project
preparation may be required under RDF Il.
42.       An interdepartmental Technical Committee has been established as
required under section 3.06 of the Credit Agreement. Certain modifications
in its membership are proposed for RDF II.
43.       Under section 3.08 the Borrower undertook to (a) provide ORDs
at all times with the necessary agricultural extension and credit services;
(b) cause ORDs to organize farmer groups for land and water development
projects; and (c) define a model contract detailing the respective rights and



ANNEX 2
Page 14
obligations of ORDs and farmers participating in land development projects.
As discussed elsewhere, additional funds were not made available for exten-
sion workers from the regular budget, and FDR has been forced to provide the
necessary funds for extension workers. This arrangement will continue under
RDF II and appropriate financial provisions are made in project costs. Farmer
groups have been formed for the various land and water development projects
and a model contract has also been drawn up. Although legally sound, the
model contract is probably too long and complicated to serve as an under-
standable and effective document between farmers and ORDs.
44.       Section 3.10 reads as follows:  "The Borrower shall cause BND,
assisted by its departments and agencies, to conduct studies on the impli-
cations of the execution of the Project to the Borrower's budget, the impact
of increased economic activity brought about by RDF and the feasibility of
collecting repayments for project costs from the farmers. The terms of
reference and the timetable for such studies shall be approved by the Associa-
tion and the Association shall have the opportunity to review the progress
of such studies and to comment on the final report." No such study has been
made, and it is not specifically proposed to make such study under RDF II,
but continuous evaluation of RDF II will also cover economic and financial
impact.
45.       Section 4.01 deals with maintaining adequate accounts and the
necessity of having such accounts audited by independent auditors. Accounts
have been maintained, but have not been audited so far.  However, arrangements
for auditing the acounts are now being made and auditors will be appointed
before negotiations. The timetable is that auditors would be appointed around
April 1976 and would undertake the actual auditing in July/August 1976, cover-
ing all accounts from 1972 until June 1976. Their report would then be
available by the end of 1976.
46.       Section 4.03 reads:  "The Borrower shall cause about 20 wells and
tubewells equipped with pumps, including hand-operated wheel-type pumps, to
be constructed...., and cause BND and the appropriate ORD to maintain records
adequate to reflect the cost, efficiency, and utility of such wells in com-
parison with the open-type wells that would.... be constructed under.... the
project." A few tubewells have been built, but no evaluation study has been
implemented. However, now that it is proposed to include both tubewell
drilling and well construction under RDF II, the necessary data can be com-
piled.
47.       Section 4.04 reads:   "(a) The Borrower shall cause BND, the appro-
priate ORD and the competent administrations to furnish to the Association
such information regarding public health and environmental sanitation as
the Association may reasonably require, and shall cooperate with and give
assistance to health experts of the Association carrying out a study
financed by the Association about the effects on the health conditions of
the populations and the measures to be taken against any possible adverse
effect. (b) The Borrower shall take the necessary steps to ensure that



ANNEX 2
Page 15
construction under the Project would not adversely affect health conditions.
Such measures shall include the control of bilhatzia snails and the larvae
of the blackfly vector of onchocerciasis in land and water development works;
and the routine treatments of wells constructed under the project against
pollution with human disease organisms." Since the signing of the Credit
Agreement, a large program of riverblindness eradication has been studied
and organized which appears to satisfy at least the issues detailed in
article 4.04(a). In addition, normal measures have been taken to safeguard
water supplies from adversely affecting health conditions, and this will
be continued.
48.       Under section 5.02(c) the Borrower undertook to inform the Asso-
ciation of any new assistance for rural development and ensure that such
assistance would not interfere with the objectives and operations of FDR.
The dialogue between IDA and Upper Volta has been a continuous one; all
aspects of rural development are regularly reviewed. However, FDR has
also managed other projects such as a USAID medium-term credit fund. There
is no objection to this and the practice should continue. However, under
RDF II, specific assurances would provide for approval by IDA of management
by FDR of any other funds. This should ensure that projects do not suffer
because of an overextension of the management capacity.
49.       In general one can conclude that the assurances requested have been
adhered to, but that auditing has been delayed and the lack of evaluation
staff in FDR has hampered the undertaking of more thorough studies and
evaluations.
F. Conclusions
50.       In spite of certain shortcomings, the first Rural Development
Project is generally regarded in Upper Volta as a successful project.
After some initial delays because of slow recruitment of personnel, late
delivery of equipment and ORDs' inexperience with the new system, the project
has aroused considerable interest as evidenced both in increases in works
performed and in the acceleration of project expenditures. These components
will be expanded under the second project. Benefits from irrigation below
dams have not materialized due to inadequate supervision. These works would
be subject to stricter appraisal criteria and control under the second
project. The higher than estimated costs of well construction have been
due largely to inefficiencies in the start-up period and with the experience
now gained, well construction under the second project is expected to be more
efficient. Village warehouses and feeder roads have been largely carried out
according to expectations.
51.       As described above, inadequate quantity and quality of personnel
is still at the root of many technical problems in project execution.



ANNEX 2
Page 16
Staffing will be strengthened under the second project. However some tech-
nical problems have also arisen out of the division of responsibilities
as agreed at the start of the project:
(a) HAER has been in charge both of carrying out works,
mostly on force account, and of supervision and
final approval of such works as its sole responsibility.
This dual responsibility, coupled with lack of person-
nel, has sometimes led to work of sub-quality standards
being approved; and
(b) the fact that the agencies more directly interested in
project implementation (ORDs and FDR) did not have
the power to prepare projects technically and execute
them, has been generally detrimental to the speed of
project preparation and execution. It should be noted
that FDR subprojects are only part of HAER's
responsibilities; the latter is also carrying out
similar projects financed by other aid sources
such as well construction by FED and FAC, and irrigation
works below dams by FED. Therefore procedures for
preparation, appraisal, supervision and evaluation
of subprojects under RDF II will adapted to this
experience. Details are in Annex 9.
52.       Although the selection criteria for subprojects (para 5.09 of
the first RDF appraisal report) clearly emphasize projects directly support-
ing farming communities, there has been, and still is, strong pressure on
FDR management to finance investments directly benefiting the ORD organiza-
tions such as office buildings and housing, warehouses or agricultral
machinery used by ORDs. The pressure is understandable as ORD budgets are
notoriously inadequate and within these, equipment budgets often get a
lower priority. Although by far the greater part of RDF investments have
gone to the direct support of farming communities, pressure for more invest-
ment and operational support of ORDs will increase under the second RDF
project, as Government contributions to CRD budgets are unlikely to be
raised substantially in the near future and, without RDF support, ORDs might
be hard put to carry out their role. Justification of such support, however,
would have to be linked directly to RDF projects benefiting farmers.
53.       In summary, the first RDF project has proven to be valuable for
rural development financing and assuming that the personnel and organiza-
tional problems are remedied, an expanded second RDF project would be
entirely justified.

UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Land and Infrastructural Development
Bottomland Development
1.        Bottomlands are small, flat areas located along temporary
"marigots" 1/ which are scattered throughout the project area. Bottomland
soils are predominantly clayey, with low permeability, and are suitable for
rice cultivation. Undeveloped bottomlands are presently used as pastures
or as extensive paddy lands. Average size of bottomlands developed under
the. First Rural Development Fund Project (RDF I) was about 6 ha (ranging
from 1 to 20 ha in the ORD of Ouagadougou).
2.        Basic Bottomland improvement under thè Second Rural Development
Fund Project (RDF II) would comprise construction of small longitudinal con-
tour bunds with cross bunds to limit individual plots to an average of about
0.2 ha. The bunds, about 1,000 meters per ha, are trapezoidal in cross sec-
tion with 0.30 m top width and 0.40 m height. Flood water would flow down-
stream, filling each consecutive strip of land between transversal bunds
crossing these through pipe culverts. Included in bottomland development
would be an initial tractor plowing; subsequent plowing, however, would be
the farmer's responsibility.
3.        Bottomland development would be the responsibility of the ORDs.
Construction of bunds would be by tractor, but farmers would participate
(compaction of fill, transportation of material), using hand tools provided
under the project. The works would permit limited control and better dis-
tribution of water, although water resources would still be very dependent
upon local climatology and hydrology. As the water discharged into the
bottomland may not be consistent with paddy requirements, growth could
still be affected by water shortages or extreme flooding. The latter could
cause bund damage although floods would be infrequent. Bund maintenance
and repair would be the responsibility of farmers, under the supervision
of ORDs, using hand tools provided for implementation of works.
4.        It is estimated that 2,700 ha could be developed, equivalent to
an annual rate of 500 ha to 800 ha. The planning, execution and supervi-
sion of bottomland development would be the responsibility of the ORDs.
Construction costs are estimated at CFAF 41,700/ha and are detailed at
Table 1.
1/   Marigots are small rivers or streams.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
Improvement/Protection of Bottomlands
5.        In some instances selected bottomlands, developed as above, could,
because of the availability of surplus water, be further improved to allow
greater water control. Also, occasions may arise when bottomlands will re-
quire protective works against flooding. In some cases protection and im-
provement may be combined. It is estimated that on up to 300 ha improve-
ments and protection may be justified or required. Improvements would
usually involve some development of the natural drainage (marigots), addi-
tional secondary drains, simple weirs across the natural course to raise the
water to field level, and supplementary irrigation channels.
6.        Improvements would be made only after careful studies and designs
had been prepared by HAER and accepted by FDR. Thereafter, in conjunction
with the ORDs, HAER would supervise the construction of the works.  Invest-
ment costs are estimated at about CFAF 150,000/ha and are detailed at
Table 2.
Small-Scale Irrigation
7.        Small-scale irrigation schemes would vary in size from 3 to 50 ha
and be based on (i) gravity irrigation below existing small dams; (ii) pump-
ing from small perennial streams, lakes or ponds; and (iii) installing wells
in depressions where the water table is close to the surface and pumping thus
relatively cheap.
8.        Main irrigation works for developing an area below a dam would com-
prise:
Flood protection dikes stretching along one or both sides of the
"marigot";
a weir across the "marigot" designed to maintain the water level
upstream consistent with gravity irrigation and to let excess
flood water flow downstream.  To meet these requirements, the weir
would have to be equipped with an intake and a spillway;
-    a head canal might be necessary to convey water to the irrigated
area;
-    an irrigation network;
-    a drainage network, if necessary, and
-    small concrete hydraulic structures.
9.        Gravity-fed irrigation has advantages over schemes depending on
pumped water, since the former avoid creating farming groups dependent on



ANNEX 4
Page 3
pump maintenance, fuel supplies, etc. Production would be centered on rice
production during the wet season with, in the dry season, probably 50% un-
der vegetables or a second rice crop. HAER would be responsible for the
planning, designing and contruction of each scheme.
10.       Investment costs are estimated at CFAF 1 million per ha.  This is
based on detailed analysis of two small irrigation projects presently under
construction (Table 3 gives details). Actual investment costs per hectare
will vary considerably depending on physical characteristics of perimeters.
In the case of pump irrigation high investments per hectare are compounded
by substantial annual operating costs. In addition to maintenance of works,
generally estimated at 3-5% of investment costs, pump operating costs could
amount to about CFAF 25,000 (US$110) per ha.
11.       HAER would prepare detailed studies for each irrigation scheme,
and the technical and appraisal studies would be submitted to IDA. The
annual program for small-scale irrigation and improvement of bottomlands
would be agreed with IDA before any works are executed by HAER.
Erosion Control Works
12.       Although the topography of the area is generally rather flat, there
is evidence of increasing erosion due to the intensity of farming and the high
runoff at the time of intense rainstorms. Erosion control works developed
under the project would have a two-fold function, (a) to reduce runoff, there-
by preventing soil loss and conserving moisture; and (b) to act as foci for
proper land and catchment area development.
13.       Erosion control works would involve construction of small contour
bunds. These would have a trapezoidal cross section, 0.40 m top width and
some 0.50 m height. The distance between two bunds would depend on slope
and soil characteristics. The entire works would be carried out by ORDs.
A preliminary topo-survey would define bund location along contour lines;
a tractor equipped with a disc plow would throw up a rough bund; reshaping
and compacting would be done by farmers, as under RDF I; labor requirements
are estimated at 19 mandays per ha. Under the project it is estimated that
9,200 ha could be developed at a rate increasing from 1,500 ha in 1976/77
to 3,000 ha in 1979/80. The cost per hectare is estimated at 14,740 CFAF
as shown in Table 4.
Wells
14.       The lack of potable water is a problem in many villages in Upper
Volta. Not only are distances great, but ponds and creeks contain poor
quality water infested with diseases. Futhermore, water supplies can run
dry at the end of the dry season. Under RDF I about 227 wells have been
constructed by four well brigades under HAER responsibility. FAC provided
technical assistance through the secondment of an expatriate brigade leader.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
15.       The technique presently used by HAER for well construction ("havage")
consists of two stages: (1) hand digging down to the aquifer in the first
year, and (2) deepening (by hand) below the aquifer, using pumping equipment
in the second year. Additional labor is provided to the brigades by villagers
on a self-help basis. The present method is not necessarily the optimal
one, although if executed properly, should produce good results. For various
reasons but mainly because of poor control over work executed, many existing
wells are not deep enough, and thus are unable to provide a sufficient volume
of water (25 liters per inhabitant per day) during the crucial period April/
May (end of the dry season), when the groundwater table is at its lowest.
Another well constuction method ("foncage continu") is being tested by HAER,
under which wells are constructed in a single stage. Under both techniques
wells are lined with concrete casings and the lower casings are perforated
(screen). Constructing or deepening wells through rocky material necessitates
the use of explosives. Main well characteristics are as follows: diameter
of 1.2 to 1.8 m, depth of 15 to 20 m. Both construction methods, if properly
executed, will produce good wells. There are certain cost advantages in
using the continuous well digging operation, which also allows more efficient
use of resources; however, this method requires experienced well brigades
and leaders. Therefore a choice between two methods is not imperative from
the technical point of view, but should be governed by practical considera-
tions.
16.       Well construction would be undertaken by the four HAER construction
brigades developed under RDF I. The expatriate provided by FAC would continue
to act as technical adviser and head of one brigade; the other three brigades
would be headed by Voltaics. Normally each brigade would be split in five
groups. Based on the assumption that work will be executed on five job sites,
one brigade should be able to construct 25 wells per year, increasing--because
of construction experience--to about 40 by the end of the project period.
17.       Four well brigades will continue to execute the well digging program.
However, at present all four brigades are too dependent on HAER's central
facilities in Ouagadougou for maintenance and other services, and much time
is spent on this and on coordination with HAER. This is inefficient and
cumbersome; under the project a coordinator for the four brigades who would
also liaise with HAER would be appointed. Furthermore, a simple prefabricated
base would be developed so that it could be easily moved elsewhere if con-
struction so required. The base would have storage and repair facilities
and be manned by a supply officer and mechanic under the responsibility of
the coordinator.
18.       Once constructed, concrete wells hardly require maintenance, but if
needed, this would be done by the beneficiaries; they will also ensure that
the well's immediate surroundings are clean at all times. HAER would handle
special repairs as required.
19.       Total costs of the well construction program are estimated at about
CFAF 535 million. This amounts to an average cost of about CFAF 1,030,000
(US$4,570) per well. Detailed costs are at Tables 5-8.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
Tubewells
20.       The Drought Relief Fund Project (Credit 442-UV) was scheduled to
be completed by June 1976, by which time 150 tubewells would have been con-
structed. Because of delays in equipment delivery and problems in operating
the drilling machine, it is expected that well drilling will not be com-
pleted until June 1977. About 120 tubewells would then have been con-
structed, and no funds would remain after completion of that program. Using
442-UV drilling equipment (with an estimated life of five years) a further
180 tubewells would be constructed under the project.
21.       Characteristics of the boreholes would be:  depth of 50 m, and
4-1/2" diameter. Each borehole would be equipped with a manual pump. Dis-
charge is difficult to estimate and would vary considerably. Very tentative
estimates place the average discharge at between 2-4 m  per hour.
22.       Execution of tubewell construction programs would be the responsi-
bility of HAER; works would be implemented in phases by three brigades re-
spectively responsible for (i) drilling works, (ii) pumping tests, and (iii)
pump installation. An efficient tubewell drilling team is now being formed
and trained with technical assistance from consultants and a well-equipped
base has been established in Dori. No change in the organization for tube-
well drilling is envisaged under RDF II. The tubewell drilling manager would
form the link and channel of communications with HAER.
23.       Tubewell maintenance is absolutely necessary for proper and continu-
ous operation of pumps. Under the project a maintenance brigade would be
formed, which would operate from the base in Dori under the responsibility
of the tubewell drilling manager. The brigade would consist of a mechanic,
driver and two laborers. It would be provided with a vehicle modified for
maintenance work and with the necessary equipment. To guarantee proper main-
tenance, it is advantageous to have the maintenance brigade under the project.
However, maintenance should be an ORD responsibility and should be transferred
to the Sahel ORD at the end of the project period.
24.       Construction details and costs are detailed at Tables 9 - 11, and
are estimated at CFAF 930,000 (US$4,130) per tubewell. These estimates do
not include equipment purchased under the Drought Relief Fund. Maintenance
costs are at Table 12.
Village Warehouses
25.       The dimensions of the village warehouses (12 m ) constructed under
RDF I were too small; under RDF II the size would be doubled. Warehouses
would be constructed of village-manufactured bricks, and the roof would con-
sist of galvanized iron sheets. Materials such as cement, galvanized sheets,
timber (for door posts and roof support), reinforcing steel (to reinforce
columns), nails and a brick press (for improved manufacturing of traditional



ANNEX 4
Page 6
bricks) could be provided by FDR through ORDs. Unskilled labor for brick
maanufacturing and construction would be provided as self-help by the vil-
lagers. The services of a mason, to assist in the more difficult construc-
tion jobs and to give guidance, and transport of supplies would be paid by
FDR. Supervision will be the responsibility of ORDs. Under the project
400 warehouses would be constructed. Costs per warehouse are estimated at
approximately CFAF 152,100. Details are in Table 13.
Village Centers
26.       As a pilot component, provision has been made under the project to
construct 20 village centers. The village centers would provide a covered
place for meetings, teaching, instruction, literacy programs, etc., and also
to have a small room for storage of medical supplies and for use by health
officials.  The meeting hall (45 m ) would have open sides except for a sur-
rounding brick wall about 1 meter high, and the small "health" room, which
would be completely walled except for the door. Materials would be delivered
to the village by FDR through ORDs concerned. Payments would also be made
for transport of materials and the services of a mason. Unskilled labor
needed for brick manufacturing and construction would be provided by the vil-
lagers as a self-help activity. Costs per center are estimated at about
CFAF 258,000. (Details are in Table 14). Before approving the construction
of a village center, FDR would ensure that no equivalent buildings (such as
schools or dispensaries) already exist in the village and that no such build-
ings are proposed to be constructed under other projects (such as the Bank's
education project) or under other bilateral aid programs.
27.       Table 15 summarizes the project targets and phasing for the imple-
mentation of the land and infrastructural development program. The estimates
must be regarded as flexible, and during implementation reallocation of funds
is likely to occur depending on the demands and results of project actions.

Footnotes
/1   Taken from a project under construction (the Dem Lake); detailed cost
estimates available at HAER.
Engineering Design
Gross and net irrigable area amount to 8.0 and 4.0 ha respectively
(176 plots of 240 m  each)   Irrigation is provided by pumping from
the lake (discharge:  65 m /hour, total manometric head:  12 m).
The water is delivered to a small balance tank which delivers a
constant flow of 18 l/s. Two main canals which are masonry lined
and with rectangular cross section, convey water to secondary
earth canals. Each plot is irrigated from a small irrigation ditch.
A controlled volume of water is provided to each plot. Excess water
is conveyed back into the lake through a drainage system. A flood
protection dike has been constructed to prevent damages from outside
runoff. Farm tracks located along secondary canals have been
designed (2 m width) to give access to all plots.
Crops
The irrigated area has been designed for the cultivation of dry season
vegetables (from October to February).

ANNEX 5
Page 1
UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Agricultural Credit
Background
1.        Upper Volta, together with five other countries 1/, is a member
of the West African Monetary Union, established in its present form since
1962. Member countries share a common currency, the CFA Franc (Franc de
la Communaute Financiere Africaine) and a common Central Bank, the Banque
Centrale des Etats de l'Afrique de l'Quest (BCEAO). Commercial banking
services in Upper Volta are provided by the Banque Internationale pour le
Commerce, l'Industrie et l'Agriculture de la Haute Volta, the Banque
Internationale de Volta and the Banque Internationale de l'Afrique de
l'Ouest. Long and medium term credit, and also agricultural credit, is
mainly financed and regulated by the National Development Bank (Banque
Nationale de Developpement - BND).
National Development Bank
2.        BND was established in 1961 as a successor to a series of state-
owned development finance institutions and in 1965, a special credit depart-
ment was set up to handle agricultural credit. BND's statutes, which are
similar to those of other development banks in French-speaking West Africa,
were approved by Decree 2/ in 1961 and amended in 1962 3/. BND's objectives,
which are defined by Public Law 4/, are to provide technical and financial
assistance to carry out all projects furthering the economic and social de-
velopment of Upper Volta.
3.        A board of directors, composed of eleven members, eight from Govern-
ment Departments, two from the CCCE of France and one from BCEAO, manages BND.
It is responsible for defining and implementing policies and credit programs.
The Board may delegate some of its powers to a limited standing committee on
which the shareholders are represented in the same proportion as on the Board.
The Board meets once a month. The General Manager is nominated by three-
fourths majority of the board, and is appointed by the Government.
1/   The five countries are Benin, Ivory Coast, Niger, Senegal, and Togo.
2/   Decree No. 82/PRES/ECNA, March 4, 1961.
3/   Decree No. 103/PRES/ECNA, April 6, 1962.
4/   Public Law No. 6/61/ANL, January 23, 1961.

4.        BND's capital is authorized as follows:
Government                                   54.55%
Caisse Centrale de Cooperation
Economique (CCCE)                             9.09%
BCEAO                                         9.09%
DEG                                           9.09%
Others                                       18.18%
100. 00%
5.        BND is responsible to the Minister of Commerce, Industry and Mines.
Accounts of BND are audited by the "Commissaire aux Comptes" of the Ministry
of Finance, and from time to time by CCCE experts. As agreed with the West
African Monetary Union, BCEAO receives monthly statements on BND's financial
and liquidity position in addition to annual reports and accounts. Internal
audit is the responsibility of the General Controller. Internal and exter-
nal audit and control seem to be adequate.
6.        Total BND staff numbers approximately 126, including a small number
of expatriates. BND personnel are well trained and qualified for their tasks.
No specially trained staff for evaluating the technical and economic aspects
of agricultural credit applications are employed, and these functions are
presently carried out by the staff of the ORDs where the credit applications
originate. However, there is some doubt whether ORD staff are sufficiently
experienced to process loan applications.
7.        Condensed balanee sheets are shown in Table 1.  Paid up capital was
CFAF 355 million, but increased to CFAF 1,100 million in August, 1974. Other
sources of funds are:
-    grants and guarantee funds from CCCE and Government, reserves pro-
visions and profit;
-    medium term loans from CCCE, the Government and BCEAO;
-    long term loans from CCCE, Government, USAID and African
Development Bank (BAD); and
-    short term loans, deposits and funds.
BND has rediscount facilities with BCEAO; medium term loans are rediscounted
for up to ten years, and short term marketing loans for one year; discount
rates were generally 3.5%, but have recently been increased to 5.5%.

Agricultural Credit
10.       Marketing loans for cotton, to the Compagnie Francaise pour le
Developpement des Fibres Textiles (CFDT) for cotton purchases, are the major
item in BND's agricultural lending. In 1973/74 they amounted to CFAF 837 mil-
lion or 76% of BND's lending. Credit for seasonal inputs amounted to CFAF 205
million in the same period. Together short term credit amounted to about 95%
of BND'S agricultural lending. BND's medium term credit for agriculture is
insignificant as can be seen from Table 5, which gives a breakdown of BND's
agricultural credit in various categories over the years. For the 1973/74
period the breakdown of BND's agricultural credit was as follows:
