ANNEX 5
Page 4
Agricultural lending over the years has increased substantially but declined
as a percentage of credit extended (see Table 5) from about 50% by the end
of the sixties to 23% in 1973/74.
11.       Basically BND lends to the 11 Regional Development Organizations
which onlend to the farmers. Short term credit applications are made in the
first instance to village committees composed of elders and the "Chef de
Terre" which approves the application.  Individual application forms are com-
pleted with the assistance of the ORD extension workers. Final approval is
given by the ORD. Before the agricultural season starts, each ORD presents
to BND its estimated requirements for seasonal short term credit for each
ORD.  Short term credit records are kept by the ORD.  Medium term credit
financing is handled on a case-by-case basis, with the applications being
reviewed by the ORD Steering Committee.  Reviewed applications and the recom-
mendations of the Committee are then sent to the BND for approval. Medium
term credit is further handled from BND headquarters in Ouagadougou where
all records are kept. Medium term credit centralization in Ouagadougou has
resulted in such problems as delay in procurement of agricultural equipment
and poor control of medium term credit repayments. Decentralization of ap-
proval and recording of medium term credit seem necessary and should be
studied by BND.
12.       Criteria established by BND for approval of medium term credit
oan applications are that an applicant must:
(a) have repaid any prior debts;
(b) belong to a precooperative association or village group;
(c)  have demonstrated in the past his willingness to follow prescribed
agricultural techniques; and
(d)  have a proven agricultural income equal to at least three times
debt service.
13.       ORDs are financially responsible to BND for all credit extended
to farmers, and ORDs have to repay even if farmers do not repay. Any sub-
sector 1/ and sector 1/ with past-due debts exceeding 10% and 20% respec-
tively, is not eligible for further credit until the situation is rectified.
BND is protected from any financial malpractices by the ORD by a general
guarantee of Government.
1/   Each ORD is divided into five or six sectors, and theee sectors are
divided into four or five subsectors.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
14.       Interest rates for short term credit are 5.5%, with repayment
within a year or a shorter period depending on the crop cycle.  Medium term
credit by BND for farm equipment is extended for two years with a down-
payment requirement of about 50%. Lending rates of BND are based on BCEAO's
rediscount rate. Since the rediscount rate was recently increased from 3.5%
to 5.5%, BND has increased its rates for agricultural credit to 8%, although
it is not yet sure if BND wishes to use differential rates for short and
medium term credit.
15.       In the past, BND experienced difficulties in recovering agricul-
tural loans, particularly medium term loans for farm implements. A Presi-
dential Decree of 1968 declared non-repayment of agricultural credit as a
national crime of the same order as tax-evasion. Since then, collection of
short term credit repayments has improved considerably, not necessarily be-
cause of the decree, but because (i) farmers who were granted loans were
required to grow a minimum area of cash crops; and (ii) responsibility for
debt collection was transferred to ORDs. The repayment performance of the
ORDs as shown in Table 6 has varied depending on their programs and poli-
cies. The present standard of loan appraisal and recovery techniques for
short term credit are such that future defaults should be minimal. As of
June 30, 1974, 94% of repayments due on short term loans had been collected.
At the same time, only 54% of all medium term repayments had been collected.
In drought years, deferment of repayment obligation is normally granted.
However, even taking this into account, repayments of medium term credit
are not completely satisfactory. This points to the fact that loan appli-
cation procedures and terms may not be well organized and adapted to the
requirements.
16.       USAID recently channeled about CFAF 63 million through FDR to far-
mers, as medium term credits for financing oxen and implements.  Farmers re-
ceived these credits at an interest rate of 5.5% with repayment to be made
over five years including one year grace period. To be eligible for such a
credit, a farmer should havé or provide one oxen himself.
Credit Under the Project
17.       Under the project it is proposed to make approximately US$0.5 mil-
lion available to BND to be used as a revolving fund for medium term agricul-
tural credit. Funds would initially be channeled from government through
FDR, but once medium term credits were made, subsequent administration would
be the responsibility of BND. Funds repaid would again be used for medium
term credit and the revolving fund would be regarded as a trust fund to be
managed by BND in accordance with rules established between Government and
BND with IDA approval.
18.       Terms of medium term credit would be 8% with a repayment period of
five years with one year grace. This is in line 1/ with the terms of the
medium-term credit fund made available to BND by USAID; this fund was managed
1/   Except for the interest rate, which was 5.5%.



ANNEX 5
Page 6
through FDR. Interest rates of the USAID fund were 5.5% and that was also
the prevailing BND interest rate at the time of appraisal.  However interest
rates were being reviewed following a change in BCEAO rediscount rate from
3.5% to 5.5%. An interest rate of 8% is proposed, but if BND sets its own
agricultural medium term credit rates differently, the project interest rate
would be reviewed accordingly.
19.       Farmers in the five ORDs on the plateau Mossi 1/ would be eligible
for medium term credit on the following conditions:
(a) a minimum down payment in cash of 10% would have to be made if the
farmer does not own one draught animal or agricultural traction
equipment;
(b)  the farmer must be known to follow advice on agricultural tech-
niques given by the extension service;
(c) the farmer must have a good repayment performance on earlier loans;
and
(d)  the farmer must have a cash income from his agricultural activities
(either from cash crops, sales of livestock or from sale of surplus
subsistence crops) estimated to be sufficient to cover more than
his repayment obligations.
These conditions and other undertakings would be spelled out in a contract to
be signed by the farmer and BND. This contract would also specify repayment
procedures. A pro forma contract would be drawn up and this would be agreed
with and approved by IDA. Although not a specific selection criterion, FDR,
in approving applications, would give preference to farmers who are already
engaged in other development activities, in other words the credit would
supplement other activities which would provide for a more complete package
of services.
20.       BND would appoint a credit officer who would be specifically re-
sponsible for the project medium term credit revolving fund.  In addition
he would study the need and organization required for medium term credit in
general.
21.       Procedures in channeling medium-term credit to farmers would be as
follows:
(a)  farmers would make applications for medium term credit to ORDs;
(b) ORDs, using the criteria laid down by FDR and BND, would screen
and initially approve applications;
(c)  BND's medium-term credit officer would visit each of the ORDs to
assist with initial approval of applications;
1/   Ouagadougou, Kaya, Yatenga, Koudougou, Koupela.



ANNEX 5
Page 7
(d) applications would be forwarded to FDR;
(e) FDR would give final approval for credit applications, consulting
BND's medium term credit officer;
(f) FDR would notify ORDs of approvals, and credit agreements, between
farmers and BND would be signed;
(g)  FDR would arrange for the necessary purchases, distributing them
to the farmers through ORDs;
(h) following disbursement, FDR would hand a list with credit amounts
per farmer to BND; the total amount would be charged to a special
BND revolving account for project medium term credit; and
(i) BND would thereafter be responsible for managing the revolving
fund, including collection of repayment.
22.       The procedures outlined above would have to be tied in with the
agricultural time-table (soil preparation to start in May). Therefore, ORDs
would approve applications throughout the year, but would complete this pro-
cess in December. Approved applications would be forwarded to FDR before
end December. FDR would review and approve applications in January, prepar-
ing its total annual program before the end of January. Subsequently, it
would organize procurement. Deliveries to farmers through ORDs would be in
March/April.
23.       Repayment by farmers would be in four equal installments after one
year grace, although the farmer would have the right to accelerate repayment.
The amount of these equal installments and dates due would be shown in the
contract signed between the farmer and BND. Repayment would be collected by
BND after harvest. Procedures would require farmers to make their paymeents
in any ORD office, where the officer in charge would give a receipt to the
farmer.  BND officials would colect repayments from these offices.  They
would also visit farmers who did not repay; ORD extension workers will not
be asked to enforce repayments. The adequacy of these arrangements will be
reviewed from time to time with BND's medium term credit officer during
supervision of RDF II.

UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Studies-/ for Economic Development of Areas Freed from Onchocerciasis
A. Introduction
1.        Joint FAO/IBRD missions 2/ visited Benin, Ghana, Mali, Togo, and
Upper Volta in June/July 1975 to: prepare terms of reference for basic studies
of areas within those countries freed from onchocerciasis, to provide for
coordination and strengthening of planning processes for preparation of the
Ten-Year Development Plan and specific projects suitable for investment by
governments, multilateral and bilateral aid agencies, and plan training of
local personnel in basic data collection, interpretation, plan preparation,
and execution.
2.        Although the countries differ in their levels of economic develop-
ment (for instance, per capita income ranges from US$240 in Ghana to US$70
in Upper Volta), there are numerous similarities. Agriculture is the pre-
dominant sector in these countries and production is declining as a result
of increased population pressure, overcultivation of lands, and erosion.
The recent drought has compounded problems. There is clearly a need for
diversification, import substitution, and improved agricultural techniques.
3.        A major cause of overcultivation and population pressure has been
the movement of people from the more fertile onchocerciasis-affected riverine
areas. In the five countries, these areas, which lie between latitudes 80
and 15  no5th and longitudes 40 east to 80 west, cover approximately
571,000 km . The reopening of these areas, with the control of the
onchocerciasis vector, would ease population pressures through resettlement,
allow for increase in agricultural production and diversification of agri-
culture, and reduce regional imbalances that presently exist. It would also
develop two major natural resources: land and water. The primary objectives
1/   This Annex is based on FAO Report WS/H 3291, October 1975, Economic
Development of Areas Freed from Onchocerciasis in Benin, Ghana, Mali,
Togo and Upper Volta, prepared by the mission mentioned in paragraph 1.
2/   The missions comprised Messrs. Burer, Kock, Osei, Otten, Sinodinos,
Weed (Bank), Groom (FAO) and Soa (Consultant). The mission was assisted
in Togo by Mr. el Kashef (FAO), in Mali by Mr. Fanfant (FAO) and in
Upper Volta by Mr. Hirsch (FAO). Mr. R. Noronha (Consultant) assisted
in preparing this report.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
of the control program is economie and social development, but humanitarian
goals are also significant.
4.        The Volta River Basin area covers nearly 700,000 km  in seven West
African countries (Benin, Ghana, Ivory Coast, Mali, Niger, Togo and Upper
Volta). The area is afflicted by onchocerciasis which brings blindness and
misery to people and inhibits development of many fertile valleys. The dis-
ease is transmitted by the bite of an infected female blackfly of the species
Simulium demnosum which thus functions as a vector for the transmission of the
disease. The onchocerciasis vector control program implemented under WHO di-
rection has been in full operation since December 1974. The effect of this
program is more or less immediate although efforts need to be sustained for 20
years to provide permanent control, and in a few areas people are already spon-
taneously returning to the fertile river valleys in anticipation of a perma-
nently controlled disease. It is imperative therefore that an inventory of
natural resources in these areas be made and plans for effective resettlement
and economic development.
5.        An important justification for the onchocerciasis control program
is humanitarian, but the primary justification is economic It is estimated
that hundreds of thousands of people could be settled in areas freed of
the disease thereby lessening population pressure on the degraded soils
of plateaux, and increasing production of food and cash crops. To prevent
uncontrolled and irreversible exploitation of one of West Africa's few remain-
ing and relatively untouched natural resources, it is essential to follow
a systematic approach to its development. Of the seven countries where
the vector control program is being executed, the Government of Ivory Coast
has itself prepared a program for development of areas to be freed fron
onchocerciasis (oncho areas) and, at this time, does not intend to seek fur-
ther assistance from UNDP or other international agencies beyond that already
requested for aerial mapping and the survey of the incidence of the tsetse
fly. The Government of Niger is also preparing its own plans for its re-
latively small oncho area and is being assisted by UNDP in this respect.
However further technical assistance will be required for the oncho areas
of Benin Republic (56,000 km ), Ghana (98,000 km ), Mali (125,000 km ), Togo
2                               2
(18,000 km ) and Upper Volta (274,000 km ).  It has been suggested that this
technical assistance might be best provided in relation with Bank/IDA proj-
ects. 1/  The remainder of this Annex deals only with economic development
of areas freed from onchocerciasis in Upper Volta, as affected by the medical
onchocerciasis control program.  This oncho area covers approximately 90% of
the national territory.
1/   For Upper Volta the proposed Second Rural Development Fund Project;
for Ghana the Upper Region Agricultural Development Project; for Mali
the Mali-Sud Agricultural Development Project.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
6.        The basic and accepted planning methodology for the oncho areas
will result in the preparation of:
(a) an inventory of natural resources (the data bank);
(b) a broad ten year development plan for priority zones of the
oncho areas (the Ten Year Development Plan); and
(c) a number of specific investment projects (project preparation).
B. The Proposed Technical Assistance Operation
General
7.        The proposed technical assistance operation would involve funding
of actions needed to achieve execution of the methodology set out in para 6.
In Upper Volta the operation would complement actions already underway with
UNDP and other financial support.
8.        Execution of the methodology would have the long term objectives
of:
(a) improving the living standards of people now living at subsist-
ence or near subsistence levels in densely populated or otherwise
in-hospitable zones, by providing opportunities for resettlement
in the relatively fertile oncho areas and/or other less populous
areas;
(b) achieving such settlement without the uncontrolled exploitation
and lack of conservation that has characterized most settlement
in West Africa; and
(c) increasing output of food, livestock, and industrial and export
crops.
9.        The operation would be carried out over a three-year period and
would consist of:
(a) completion of the data bank;
(b) preparation of a Ten-Year Development Plan; and
(c)  preparation ot [,ivestrnent projects for early submission to ex-
ternal financing agencies and execution in the first four years
of the Development Plan.
10.       The approach proposed for planning developmnt of the oncho areas
does not indicaate that the resulting investment projects will be highly
complex or costly. Government wishes to ensure that a maximum number of



ANNEX 6
Page 4
people will benefit from oncho area development and will seek to achieve
this by providing settlers with minimum assistance, commensurate with
achieving a satisfactory in-flow of people, and effective conservation
measures. Therefore good basic data and planning are important.
Detailed Features
11.       Çompletion of the Data Bank.  Upper Volta has a development pro-
gram that extends to specific oncho afflicted areas located in the valleys
of the Black, Red and White Volta Rivers. The "Autorite des Amenagements
des vallees de Volta" (AVV) is even executing projects of one type or
another in these areas (AVV areas). However, Government acknowledges the
need for better overall planning and has agreed that a data bank compris-
ing up-to-date knowledge of the oncho areas, their resources and poten-
tialities is of national interest. It is also agreed that a Development
Plan for these areas conceived on the basis of a sound knowledge and
analysis of the area's fundamental potentialities is necessary both as a
planning tool and to facilitate obtaining external financial assistance.
The principal elements of a data bank needed for future development plan-
ning purposes are: (a) present land use; (b) land suitability classifica-
tion (physical and chemical properties of soils and social and economic
parameters would be used to determine the economic value of different
soil types); (c) climate; (d) surface and groundwater resources and poten-
tials; and (e) socio-economic data on: population, land tenure and social
organization and key data relating to agriculture, livestock, forestry and
fishery.
12.       Many studies and surveys have already been made in the oncho areas.
For example, Office de la Recherche Scientifique et Technique d'Outre-Mer
(ORSTROM) has prepared soil maps and Institut Geographique National has
produced topographic maps. Raw climatological data are available in Paris
and London. For over a decade the oncho area has also been covered by
LANDSAT satellites and accumulated data are on computer tapes. A consider-
able amount of social and economic data exists. All these data, although
scattered in Africa, Europe and elsewhere, are retrievable, and conse-
quently, constituting the data bank would involve: (a) the retrieval of
existing data, (b) its collation, and (c) supplementation where gaps exist
through new surveys and studies carried out specifically for this purpose.
When complete the data bank should provide a profile of any significant
subject relating to the oncho areas. This profile would provide not merely
gross categories of information but also show the types of local variations
likely to assist or constrain developmental activities.
13.       Preparation of a Ten-Year Development Plan.  This would be prepared
based on material from the data bank. The plan would define specific zones
suitable for priority development over the next decade and would be divided
into phases, each consisting of one or more investment projects. Critical
for plan preparation is the establishment by Government of parameters and
criteria for determining zone priorities and the type of projects it wishes



ANNEX 6
Page 5
to promote. Factors to be considered in determining priorities should in-
clude: (i) resource potential; (ii) population pressure in potential emmigra-
tion areas; (iii) existence of social and cultural conflicts between ethnic
groups in overpopulated areas; and (iv) per capita settlement costs. The Plan
would incorporate existing Government plans for AVV's oncho areas and would
also take into account the national development plan presently being prepared.
14.       Preparation of Investment Projects.  Detailed preparation of proj-
ects would not commence before a thorough review of the Development Plan
by Government and potential donors have been sought. However, identification
of projects would begin at the same time as work on the Ten-Year Development
Plan is initiated.
C. Execution of the Technical Assistance Operation
15.       Technical assistance would be provided by appropriately qualified
consulting firms to ensure that the operation, designed to lead to early
emergence of investment proposals would be completed within the time limits
specified. There is local expertise (including institutions) qualified in
varying degrees to do part of the work and consulting firms submitting offers
would be required to assess the availability of local expertise and to
specify how they will mobilize and employ it. It is, however, important
to note that much of the work entails assembly of data available only out-
side West Africa.
The Data Bank
16.       Completion of the data bank would require three principal opera-
tions:
(i) a land use survey and mapping and associated climatic
and hydrological studies;
(ii)  a land suitability survey and mapping; and
(iii) a social and economic survey.
17.       Land Use Survey and Mapping.  This survey would rely primarily on
satellite remote sensing, supplemented where necessary by existing photo-
graphy, and ground and aerial observations.  Given the highly specialized
nature of this work, and the geographic homogeneity of the oncho areas, it
would be preferable that a single contract be let for this purpose. 1/ In
addition data need to be completed for climatology and for hydrology.
1/   Draft terms of reference for various studies are in FAO Report WS/l 3291
(see footnote 1, page 1).



ANNEX 6
Page 6
18.       Land Suitability Survey and Mapping.  An individual contract would
be let for this work in Upper Volta.
19.       Social and Economic Survey.  An individual contract would be let
for this work.
20.       While the land suitability, and social and economic survey work
would be let as distinct contracts, consulting firms would be permitted to
bid for any number of these contracts. It would ensure maximum competition
between consulting firms thereby guaranteeing the quality of the consultants'
personnel offered. An operation calendar showing the scheduling of the
various surveys is in Chart 15624, attached to this Annex. It shows that
surveys would be completed within a six-month period after which the planning
process would commence.
Ten-Year Development Plan (the Plan)
21.       A special Development Planning Group would be established to be
responsible for planning economic development of the oncho areas. The main
task of the Planning Group would be the preparation of the Ten-Year Develop-
ment Plan.
22.       The Planning Group would have a permanent chief (Senior Oncho Areas
Planning Advisor), either a national, if available, or recruited internation-
ally. The terms of reference and the qualifications of the Senior Oncho
Areas Planning Advisor would be satisfactory to IDA. In estimating costs it
has been assumed that the Senior Oncho Areas Planning Advisor will be re-
cruited internationally for a three-year period.
23.       The Planning Group would further consist of a team of specialists
comprising a rural development planner, an agriculturalist/agro-economist,
and a civil engineer. Provision has also been made for short-term employment
of other specialists that may be required. The services of these specialists
would be obtained under contracts with appropriately qualified consulting
firms. The Planning Group would be established as soon as results from the
land use survey would be available. It is estimated that the first draft of
the Plan would be available within six months after establishment of the
Planning Group. The team of specialists would be employed during this period,
and would return a year later to revise and update the Plan.
Preparation of Investment Projects
24.       Following Plan preparation, development priorities should be estab-
lished and projects prepared. The technical assistance proposed includes
funds for two to three contracts with consultants for the preparation of
feasibility studies, which would be suitable for international aid agencies.
Responsibility for Implementation
25.       To complete the data bank, additional maps, surveys and studies
are required to be executed by highly specialized consultant services together



ANNEX 6
Page 7
with local expertise presently available in rural development and resettle-
ment projects. Since both types of projects fall under the newly-established
Ministry of Rural Development, it is proposed that completion of the data
bank would be carried out under the tutelage of this Ministry. IDA staff
will contribute substantially and assist Government in drawing up terms of
reference and in the selection of suitable consultancy firms. Consultants
should have qualifications and be appointed on terms and conditions satis-
factory to IDA.
26.       To design the Ten-Year Development Plan, which is of national
interest, planning capability would be strengthened in the Ministry of Plan-
ning by the long-term appointment of a Senior Advisor (Senior Oncho Areas
Planning Advisor), assisted by short-term specialists, and backed up by con-
sultants for specific studies. The Ten-Year Development Plan would be pre-
pared by the Ministry of Planning after consultation with the Ministry of
Rural Development. Provision has also been made for consultant services
to prepare feasibility studies for specific investment proposals, directly
related to development of areas located in the various ORDs and/or falling
under the jurisdiction of AVV. The primary responsibility for executing
these studies would belong to the Ministry of Rural Development, which can
delegate further responsibilities to AVV or an ORD.
27.       AVV is responsible for the economic development of well-delineated
areas along the Volta Rivers. Recently consultants, financed by UNDP with
the Bank as executing agency, reported on AVV's organization, its future role
and related organizational and financial requirements. 1/ This report is
presently being studied by Government and its implications being discussed
with IDA. During negotiations assurances were obtained that Government
would present to IDA not later than March 31, 1977 its proposals with respect
to detailed tasks and specific responsibilities of its agencies which will be
responsible for the execution of development projects in the areas freed from
onchocerciasis.
28.       Overall responsibility for implementation of the oncho studies
would rest with the Coordinating Committee for Rural Development. For
practical purposes no new financing channel will be created and disburse-
ments will be channelled through FDR.
29.       The Senior Oncho Areas Planning Advisor would be responsible to
the Minister of Planning. He and other specialists of the planning team
would work closely with AVV, which is the only local organization that has
adequate experience on resettlement schemes.
D. Costs and Financing
30.       Costs are estimated at about US$1.8 million including contingen-
cies. Cost estimates are summarized in Table 1 and detailed in Tables 2-7.
1/   Bereschot, Moret, Bosboom; see also their Report R 6082/292 dated
January 1976.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
31.       IDA would finance 100% of total cost of expatriate personnel,
100% of the foreign expenditures of consultancy services, and 50% of other
local expenditures; IDA would also assist in the recruitment of the Senior
Oncho Areas Planning Advisor and assist in drawing up terms of reference
for consultants.
E. Justification
32.       The similium eradication program itself has been shown to be eco-
nomically justified, on the assumption that even in the absence of speci-
fic, planned projects, there would be spontaneous migration of people from
the over-populated plateaux to the fertile river valleys freed from oncho-
cerciasis, where they would engage in traditional agriculture. Under such
conditions, and assuming a pessimistic-optimistic range for costs of infra-
structure, value of output and rate of migration, the incremental value
of the output from such areas exceeded the costs of eradication plus infra-
structure; the economic rate of return was calculated as ranging between
8% and 16%. The justification for the investments proposed for the economic
development of the oncho areas depends on the demonstration that the planning
exercise will result in national investments which would lead to benefits
(principally in the form of higher agricultural production) exceeding the
costs of both the planning and the subsequent investments. A numerical de-
monstration of this assertion in the usual manner (i.e., economic rate of
return or cost-benefit ratio) would be merely a formal exercise, since moder-
nizing agricultural production techniques in that region has consistently
led to improvements in yields which, translated into economic rates of re-
turn, have been of the order of 15% to 25% and even higher. The major con-
straint to a successful and rapid transformation of traditional agriculture
has been lack of information to permit a more accurate prediction of the
likely response of soil, people, farming systems, etc., to a given package
of improvements. The rationale for planning is precisely that it increases
the likelihood of f inding workable combinations of technological improve-
ments.
33.       Land to be freed from onchocerciasis infestation represents
almost the last remaining significant area of West Africa which man has not
yet exploited. Proper conservation, reserve protection and renewal are re-
quired to safeguard this asset, but this will be impossible without appro-
priate planning. The cost of the proposed technical assistance represents
only a minute fraction of any reasonable estimate of the value of the land
and is insignificant in comparison with benefits that should emerge from
well-conceived projects for development of the oncho areas.

SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Climatology, Surface and Groundwater Studies
A.   Climatology
The collection, analysis and presentation of climatological features
is assumed to form part of a consultant contract for land use surveys. Data
from weather satellites combined with ground observations and soil data would
make it possible to estimate the potential production of areas with forest,
food and fiber. In addition to climatological maps on the same scale as the
land-use maps, a climatological report should be produced, describing: cli-
matological characteristics, the relationship between weather and yield po-
tential in the past ten years, and a weather-yield forecast for the forth-
coming three years. Additional cost for climatological mapping is estimated
at US$45,000.
B.   Surface and Groundwater Studies
To have a better knowledge of surface and groundwater potential
for the project area in the various countries, it is believed that in the
estimated time span of 6 months for completion of the data bank, explora-
tory drillings cannot be carried out. It is therefore proposed to use the
technique of remote sensing to delimit the extent of relevant water bodies
in dry and wet season, taking into account the existing data, and to prepare
water exploration guide maps, indicating priority areas with a high proba-
bility of finding water sources. This would be carried out in the context
of a package deal for the preparation of land use maps by means of remote
sensing. Additional costs would be estimated at about US$55,000 for this
study. The final report should include a review and updated compilation of
existing data and data collected by consultants, together with maps drawn to
the same uniform scale as specified in the terms of reference for land use
specifications.

UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Organization and Management
General
1.        The main institutions concerned with Project implementation are:
(a) Rural-Development Fund (FDR),
(b) Rural Development Works Department (HAER), of the Ministry of Rural
Development, and the
(c) Regional Development Organizations (ORDs).
In addition there is an Interministerial Technical Committee which is respon-
sible for coordination and supervision of FDR activities.
2.        The studies for economic development of areas freed from
Onchocerciasis are a specific part of the project. Responsibilities for
executing this part of the project are in Annex 6.
Rural Development Fund (FDR)
3.        FDR will continue to be ultimately responsible for project execu-
tion. Under the project, it would be strengthened mainly in evaluation and
accounting to effectively manage the greatly increased scope of the second
fund.
4.        Responsibilities of FDR staff would include the following:
(a) assisting ORDs and HAER if necessary, in identifying and preparing
subprojects (pre-appraisal);
(b) appraising subproject proposals, in particular technical standards
of proposed works, degree of farmers' support, organizational
arrangements and implementation schedule, incremental production
to be expected, economic benefits and provisions for adequate main-
tenance;
(c) technical inspection upon completion ("reception "); together with
HAER;
(d) evaluating projects after completion, especially impact on agricul-
tural production as well as adequacy of maintenance, services pro-
vided by ORDs to support project investments;



ANNEX 9
Page 2
(e) purchasing equipment and materials needed for project execution;
(f)  administering all FDR accounts, including control on accounts
maintained by ORDs for FDR subprojects, and submitting audited
annual accounts to IDA;
(g)  preparing annual development programs, accompanied by budgets,
and submitting them for approval to the Interministerial Technical
Committee;
(h) preparing annual reports and semi-annual progress reports for sub-
mission to the Interministerial Technical Committee, to the Coor-
dination Committee on Rural Development, to the Director General
of BND, and to IDA; and
(i) preparing reimbursement applications for submission to IDA and
other external sources of finance.
5.        Present FDR staff consists of a director, an assistant accountant,
a secretary and two drivers. Staffing will be considerably strengthened by
appointing two evaluation officers, an accountant, a supply officer, two
additional drivers and an additional secretary.
6.        FDR is accommodated in the BND building.  FDR pays no rent for this
accommodation, but BND levies a 1% service charge on all FDR transactions.
Since present accommodation will be too small for an expanded FDR, accommoda-
tion will be rented elsewhere in Ouagadougou by the second half of 1976 and
additional office staff (messengers, cleaners, watchman) will have to be
hired. Project costs include the appropriate provision for these expenditures
and the 1% service charge would be abolished after FDR has rented its own
office.
7.        FDR accounts have not yet been audited.  However, before negotia-
tions, auditors would be appointed. It is envisaged that auditing will take
place in July/August 1976 and that the auditors' report will be available by
the end of December 1976. The auditors will also advise FDR about its account-
ing system, which needs to be improved to allow better stock inventory and more
detailed analysis of costs. The appointment of an FDR accountant and supply
officer would safeguard implementation of these accounting improvements.
8.        Two expatriate evaluation officers would be appointed to assist FDR
in appraisal and evaluation. Few data were collected under RDF I, and con-
siderable improvements are required for monitoring and evaluation. The eval-
uation officers would have the following qualifications:
(a) agricultural economist, very experienced in all aspects of agri-
culture, socio-economic evaluation, appraisal techniques, and plan-
ning and programming; some experience with livestock and agricul-
tural processing would be useful;



ANNEX 9
Page 3
(b)  rural engineer, with some economie background, very experienced
in land and water development projects, such as those proposed
under this project, and in well and tubewell construction. His
agricultural and economic background should be sufficient to enable
him to participate fully in evaluation;
Both officers would work under the Director of FDR. They would be responsible
for appraisal and evaluation of subprojects, and assist ORDs where necessary
in identification. They would assist with planning and programming of FDR
activities, although this would be the main responsibility of the Director.
They would also organize in collaboration with ORDs a system to monitor and
evaluate subproject results. The rural engineer would be specifically re-
sponsible for technical inspection together with HAER. The first task of the
evaluation officers would be to prepare an evaluation report of RDF I.
9.        FDR would prepare reports and budgets as follows:
(a) Budgets would be prepared semi-annually to allow their use for
fiscal years (January to December) and project years (July to
June). Budgets would be submitted to the Interministerial Tech-
nical Committee for approval and would be subsequently forwarded
to IDA for monitoring.
(b) Statements of project expenditures and use of funds will be pre-
pared twice a year (as of end December, and as of end June);
they would be forwarded to IDA together with the progress reports.
(c) Progress reports, in a form satisfactory to IDA, would be prepared
twice a year. They would be submitted to IDA and also to the
Interministerial Technical Committee in January and July of each
year.
(d) An annual detailed report would be prepared, as presently, which
should cover the project year. It should be available three months
after the end of the project year. This report would be widely
distributed and among the recipients would be IDA, other donors,
the Interministerial Technical Committee, and the Coordinating
Committee for Rural Development.
(e) Audited accounts and the auditors' report in a form satisfactory
to IDA would be forwarded to IDA within four months after the
end of the project year.  The first auditors' report is likely
to be available by the end of 1976 (see para. 7) and would
cover the period 1972 to June 30, 1976.
10.       FDR has proven to be an efficient channel for financing of scattered
agricultural investments. This has been recognized by other donors such as
USAID which asked FDR to manage its medium-term credit fund. This is an
efficient use of scarce management resources. However, different donors have



ANNEX 9
Page 4
different procedures (especially for procurement and disbursements) which might
not simplify joint management of separate project funds for which further addi-
tional staffing is likely to be needed. Therefore, although in principal the
use of FDR as a channel for investments by other donors should be encouraged,
the implications should be carefully studied, and IDA agreement for this would
have to be obtained.
Interministerial Technical Committee
11.       It was a condition of effectiveness for Credit 317-UV that an Inter-
ministerial Technical Committee would be established. This committee was
established 1/ with a large membership 2/ as follows: the Minister of Rural
Development (Chairman), a representative of the Prime Minister's office,
Director General of Education, Director General of Health, Director of Agri-
cultural Services Department, Director of HAER, Director of Livestock Depart-
ment, Director of Forestry Department, Director of Public Works Department,
Director of BND, Director of the Treasury, Director of the Budget, a repre-
sentative of the Ministry of Planning, Director of Social Affairs, and the
Director of FDR. This committee being too large met only sporadically in
the past, but has served as a useful forum to promote FDR. This is hardly re-
quired anymore and can further be better handled by the recently established
Coordinating Committee for Rural Development.
12.       An Interministerial Technical Committee was also set up in 1974 3/
for the Drought Relief Fund Project (Credit 442-UV). Its membership is almost
identical to the Interministerial Technical Committee which was established for
FDR with the exception of the Minister of Commerce, Industrial Development and
Mining who is Vice President of the "drought committee" and the representative
of the Prime Minister's Office who is not a member of the "drought committee."
13.       The two interministerial technical committees would be abolished and
one new interministerial technical committee created for supervision of FDR.
Membership of the new committee would be as follows:
Chairman: Minister of Rural Development
Members:   The Minister of Planning, or a senior representative
Director of the Permanent Secretariat for ORDs
Director of BND
Director of HAER
Director of Agricultural Services
Director of the Budget
Director of the Treasury
Secretary: Director of FDR
1/   Decret No. 72-142/PM/AGRI/EL dated July 21, 1972.
2/   The committee was established in 1972 and since that time Government
has been reorganized various times. Nomenclature used in this
paragraph does not reflect the edict, but present Government structure.
3/   Decret No. 74/902/PRES/PL-DR-E dated March 28, 1974.



ANNEX 9
Page 5
Two additional members such as the Director of the Livestock Department,
and the Director of the Forestry Department would be co-opted if FDR would
undertake activities in these fields. The committee would meet at least
twice a year, but preferably quarterly. Since the Director of the Permanent
Secretariat for ORDs is a member of the committee he will decide if there
are any policy issues which need to be referred to the Coordinating Committee
for Rural Development.
14.       In addition to this official committee, the Director of FDR would
meet at least quarterly with the Directors of the five ORDs on the plateau
Mossi. 1/ However this would not be an official committee.
Rural Development Works Department (HAER)
15.       Under RDF I, HAER was responsible for all engineering and design,
and subsequently for execution of subprojects. This was clearly a constraint
since HAER executive capacity is limited. Under the project main responsibil-
ity for traditional bottomland development, erosion control works, and village
buildings would rest with ORDs and HAER would concentrate on technically more
complex works such as small-scale irrigation and improved bottomland develop-
ment and on well construction and tubewell drilling. However, HAER is the
technical department in the Ministry of Rural Development, and as such has to
safeguard technical standards nationwide. Therefore, HAER would be responsi-
ble for:
(a) guidelines and standards for engineering and design by other organ-
izations; and
(b) technical inspection upon completion ("reception") together with
FDR.
16.       Although HAER was fully staffed during RDF I at a certain point in
time, it was mostly understaffed and lacked experienced personnel, which
seriously limited its executing capability. Under the project FAC will pro-
vide expatriate technical assistance to HAER as follows:
(a) senior rural engineer, who would be the technical advisor to the
FDR coordinator in HAER;
(b) junior rural engineer who would be responsible for small-scale
irrigation and improved bottomlands;
1/   Ouagadougou, Koupela, Kaya, Yatenga, Koudougou.



ANNEX 9
Page 6
(c) topographical engineer who would be responsible for evaluating
topographical studies and for overall guidance to ORDs regarding
topographical studies and land surveys; and
(d) well brigade leader.
17.       An assistant to the Director of HAER will also be appointed to
coordinate all FDR activities in HAER. He will provide and arrange the
necessary administrative and logistic support for project execution by HAER.
He will provide the channel of communication between HAER and FDR, and will
also be responsible for HAER reporting.
18.       Tubewell drilling is executed and managed from a base in Dori pre-
sently by an expatriate, but a Voltaic counterpart is being trained. The
manager undertakes the necessary coordination with HAER. No similar organiza-
tion exists for well construction. In order not to necessitate each well
brigade leader to communicate separately with HAER and to speed up administra-
tive support, a coordinator for the four well brigades would be appointed to be
responsible for the FDR-financed well construction program, and for providing
the channel of communication with HAER. A small base, which would be easy
to relocate, would be created for storage and maintenance so as to reduce time-
consuming reliance on faraway central HAER facilities in Ouagadougou. The
base would be staffed by a supply officer and mechanic.
19.       The project provides finance for the above HAER staff and related
equipment and operating costs (details are in Annex 7, Tables 3, 4 and 5).
In addition, two HAER topographical brigades for land survey work will be
funded under the project.
Regional Development Organizations (ORDs)
20.       ORDs have recently been reorganized.  There are still shortages
of staff, procedures have to be further detailed and accounting is weak.
Given these constraints ORDs perform reasonably well. Although ORDs un-
doubtedly can use more support, this has to be provided by Government within
the framework of the Permanent Secretariat for ORDs. Under the project only
specific support related to project investments will be provided.
21.       The role of ORDs in project execution would be expanded under
RDF II. In addition to existing tasks of identification and supervision
subprojects, and provision of supporting services to project beneficiaries,
ORDs would also undertake engineering, design and execution of simple works
(bottomlands and erosion control works) and of village buildings. There-
fore, ORD land development sections will be strengthened under the project
by:
(a) providing services of expatriate volunteers at, the junior
topographical engineer ]evel to supervise land survey work and
land development work;



ANNEX 9
Page 7
(b) providing topographical brigades to do land survey work;
and
(c) providing tractors for use in land development works.
The actual situation in this respect in October 1975 was as follows: in
Ouagadougou and Koupela, ORD volunteers were already assisting with topo-
graphical work. The Ouagadougou ORD had already organized a topographical
brigade although it was badly equipped. To remedy this, three more volunteers
were requested from the Dutch Government and arrived in Upper Volta by the
end of December 1975. The project will finance staff of four topographical
brigades, and provide topo equipment and tractors to the five ORDs on the
plateau Mossi.
22.       Ineffective extension and poor maintenance of land and water devel-
opment projects have, as noted in Annex 2, mainly resulted from lack of per-
sonnel. Therefore, it was decided to recruit and finance extension workers
directly under RDF I. This practice will be continued under RDF II. The
number of extension workers based on estimates (detailed in Table 1) which
relate number of extension workers to the development program of the project,
to be financed would be as follows:
1975/76      40
1976/77      60
1977/78     100
1978/79     150
1979/80     200
23.       Project investments are part of ORDs' overall investment program
and should be incorporated in that program as such. Therefore, ORDs have a
specific responsibility to ensure that this is the case. This is not the
responsibility of FDR, although they obviously "as bankers" have an interest
in the good performance of ORDs and may therefore discuss the relation between
FDR investments and the ORDs' overall development program from time to time.
This applies even more emphatically to the land and water development projects
which have to fit within regional land planning. It makes no sense to develop
a small area without thinking about wider regional land planning, especially
in the case of erosion control water. Land development sections of ORDs
should take this into account in making detailed proposals for land develop-
ment subprojects. The data collection for studies for economic development
of areas freed from onchocerciasis will cover a large part of Upper Volta and
land development sections should use the data (especially aerial photography
might be useful) so made available for their land planning purposes.
24.       ORDs will provide extension, credit and input supply services to
participants on subprojects and will also undertake supervision of main-
tenance of completed subprojects. Therefore, ORDs basically would ensure
the success of the various subprojects. It is important that both ORDs
and Government realize this and that adequate funds and support be provided
to ORDs to enable them to ensure successful and efficient operation and main-
tenance on subprojects.



ANNEX 9
Page 8
Responsibilities in the Subproject Cycle
25.       A schedule of responsibilities in the subproject cycle is
attached as Table 2, which should be self-explanatory. A schedule of
responsibilities for project agricultural credit is attached as Table 3,
which also should be self-explanatory but further details are in Annex 5.
Responsibilities with regard to executing the training component are de-
tailed in Annex 10.
26.       In initial identification of subprojects, two basic criteria
would be applied. These are that the subproject would be:
(a) of a nature for which strong farmers' demand or support exists
to the extent that - if construction becomes necessary -
unskilled labor would be freely provided; and
(b)  small-scale, requiring onlyai,nmple organizational arrangements
+ ''ftiinimum equipment and materîfà-t, for construction.
Having met these criteria, priority would be given to projects which would
enlarge or improve upon existing investments seeking to improve agricultural
production and increase rural incomes.
27.       Subprojects that would pass the above test would be further
elaborated and appraised by FDR. In the case of traditional bottomlands,
erosion control works and village buildings, in-depth appraisal of each sub-
project would not be necessary; for traditional bottomlands and erosion con-
trol, FDR would satisfy itself that information is complete (farmers' agree-
ment, adequate design, cost estimates, number and size of holdings, timing
of construction, adequate organizational arrangements and extension) and that
the works would fall within the "standard" type on which experience has al-
ready been gained. For village warehouses, FDR would in addition require
that they be built only if a sufficient number of farmers already use fertil-
izer, seeds or insecticides or that such demand is likely to develop in re-
lation to other subprojects (bottomlands, irrigation, erosion control) or
that storage demand for crop is substantial. As far as village centers are
concerned, FDR will also ensure that no similar buildings already exist or
are being planned under other projects or programs. Improved bottomlands
and irrigation schemes would be subject to full appraisal by FDR. The annual
/  rogram of investments in improved bottomlands and irrigation schemes would
t'<appo ~For new types o   proje
~nanced byFDR under the category of "unidentified projects" the proposals
would be appraised, fully documented and submitted to IDA for approval.1 No
---Fîna ncaa6-d--eonôuId bappmI-d* ahfo`r -wèlZ coïïs'iiïr-t-c , the
criteria for village selection adopted under the RDF I (number of villagers
being served by the proposed well and extent - or security - of alternative
water sources) would continue to apply. An additional factor would, however,
be the extent to which villagers own cattle which are using existing water
points in or around the village throughout the year. Criteria for farmers'
eligibility for medium-term credit are described in Annex 5; ORDs would
group farmers' credit requests for submission to FDR.



ANNEX 9
Page 9
28.       All subproject proposals would be grouped into a development
program to be submitted each year by FDR to the Interministerial Technical
Committee for approval.
29.       Monitoring and evaluation of results obtained from investments in
subprojects are important to determine the benefits and effectiveness of the
"Fund." Under RDF I, this was not actively pursued due to manpower con-
straints.  However, under RDF II, a monitoring and evaluation system will be
established in FDR which will be run by two evaluation officers to be
appointed under UNDP assistance. This will cover all investments made under
RDF I and will be expanded from year to year to cover investments made under
RDF II. FDR will ultimately be responsible for evaluation, but ORDs, by
accepting investments from FDR, will undertake to collect all necessary data
for this purpose.
30.       The monitoring and evaluation system would be maintained at ORD
and/or HAÉR, duplicatea at-I1R neadquàrters.A file would be established
at the time o  camp etion for each subproject and would contain the major
physical and cost characteristics. After completion, further data would be
collected by ORD extension workers, which should measure the various bene-
fits of the subproject. Summary tabulations would be prepared quarterly
by ORDs and forwarded to FDR, together with the supporting details. FDR
would further process these data, and would, based on a 10% sample of all
subprojects ever financed by FDR, undertake actual verification. The annual
report of FDR would contain a separate volume which would detail evaluation
results. In addition, FDR would make specific subproject oriented evalua-
tion studies.
31.       Data to be collected would be determined by FDR.  However, it is
obvious that data collection would be different for "productive" versus "non-
productive" investment. Whereas it may always be possible to compare results
of productive investment with a sample of those farmers without investment,
such comparison would not be meaningful for nonproductive investment. In
order to obtain measurable benefits for nonproductive investments, one should
therefore collect data from the prospective beneficiaries prior to investment
and compare the same data after investment.
32.       Data collection requirements will be specified by FDR.  A few will
be highlighted below:
Evaluation data for productive investments:
(a) completion data (costs, physical characteristics);
(b)  measurement of inputs and agricultural supporting services pro-
vided by the ORDs;
(c)  measurement of output (yields and income);
(d) measurement of maintenance performance;



ANNEX 9
Page 10
(e)  measurement of farmer response to other agricultural supporting
services (e.g., credit) not directly related to the subproject;
(f)  data sample collection of farmers not benefiting from project in-
vestments.
Evaluation data for "nonproductive" investments:
(a) before investment data (for wells, for example, existing sources
of water supply in various seasons, number of livestock, water
supply related data such as health and use of water);
(b) after investment data (identical);
(c) determination of which before investment sources are still being
used and for what purposes;
(d) measurement of maintenance performance;
(e) measurement of perceived benefits by beneficiaries.
It is obvious that the above indicates that only few data are needed, and will
have to be refined and expanded in a continuous dialogue between ORDs and FDR
and between FDR and IDA.

Training
1.        General.  Lack of trained and experienced personnel remains a
major constraint to effective project implementation in Upper Volta. Com-
petent expatriate technical assistance is considered necessary in the fields
of evaluation, engineering and topographical survey.
2.        Training under the Project.  The training component under RDF
II 1/ has been determined by taking into account:
(a) the specific staff requirements of RDF II; and
(b) ongoing training activities.
and would comprise:
(a) training and scholarships for Voltaic counterparts of expatriate
project officers (evaluation officers, engineers, technicians);
(b) consultancy services to determine future training needs of exten-
sion workers and to design a master plan for their training;
and
(c) scholarships for short rural development courses for ORD staff
or villagers responsible for small rural development projects.
3.        The following scholarships for Voltaic counterparts would be pro-
vided under the project:
-    three scholarships for rural engineers who would study for three years
at the International School for Rural Engineers at Ouagadougou;
-    twelve scholarships for agricultural technicians who would study for
two years at the International School for Agricultural Technicians
near Ouagadougou; and
-    six scholarships for "Agents des Travaux Agricoles" who would study
at the Matourkou College near Bobo-Dioulasso.
In addition Voltaic evaluation officers would be trained under UNDP technical
assistance, which provides for 96 manmonths of training scholarships. However,
although scholarships obviously would be for agro-economic and technical eval-
uation, no school or institute where training would take place has yet been
selected.
1/   No training component was included under RDF I.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
4.        International School for Rural Engineers (Ecole Inter-Etats
d'Ingenieurs de l'Equipement Rural). The school is located in Ouagadougou,
and was established in 1968 by 13 African countries to provide training for
future high level officials responsible for rural engineering. Candidates
must have two years of university study, including irrigation, or pass a com-
petitive entrance examination. Study takes three years and main topics in-
clude: economics, planning procedures, infrastructure, health, energy, and
water resource management.
5.        International School for Agricultural Technicians (Ecole Inter-
Etats des Techniciens Superieurs de l'Hydraulique et de l'Equipement Rural
de Kamboinse). The school was established in 1965 and aims at providing
students with practical background in agricultural development. Candidates
must have a "baccalaureat" 1/ or pass a competitive entrance examination.
Two-year courses are given and among the topics studied are hydraulics,
topography and rural works. The School's Board of Directors is the same
as for the International School for Rural Engineers. Training capacity of
the school will be expanded from the present 30 students to 80 students.
6.        Matourkou.  Training facilities in Matourkou include:
(a) training of extension workers (one year); and
(b) training of "Agents des Travaux Agricoles" (four years).
7.        The Matourkou College provides training for "Agents des Travaux
Agricoles." Courses take four years to complete. A "Brevet d'Enseignement
Agricole" concludes the first three years of study. The first four months
of the fourth year are used for on-the-job training and the remainder of
the fourth year is used for general courses such as administration, manage-
ment and community development.  After final examinations the "Brevet d'Agent
Technique" is issued. Admission level is "Brevet d'Enseignement Premier
Cycle." 2/ There is no specialization during the first two years, but after-
wards students specialize in either agriculture, livestock, or forestry and
water resources.
8.        CESAO.  This center was established in 1960 and has since been
assisted by various aid agencies. Originally its purpose was to provide
two years education in economics and sociology. Since the advent of the
70s, however, foeus has shifted towards shorter courses directed towards
understanding of rural development, mainly at the village and lower project
executive levels. CESAO offers two types of courses: (a) "cycles", three-
month courses at the center for about 15 to 20 participants from different
countries in West Africa, but about half come from Upper Volta; and (b)
"sessions", courses of about one to two weeks given at the center or in
the field.
1/   "Baccalaureat" is the French secondary school completion degree.
2/   A certificate obtained at the end of the lower secondary cycle of
-    education.



ANNEX 10
Page 3
9.        Execution of Training Component.  The Director of FDR would
in principle be responsible for execution of the training component.
-    Candidates for scholarships at the International School for Rural
Engineering and the International School for Agricultural Tech-
nicians would be selected by the Director of FDR and the Director
of HAER.
-    Candidates for scholarships at the Matourkou College would be
selected by the Director of FDR in consultation with the Director
of HAER and the Directors of ORDs.
-    Candidates for evaluation training would be selected by the
Director of FDR in consultation with UNDP.
-    Candidates for courses at CESAO would be selected by the Director
of FDR in consultation with Directors of ORDs but also after con-
sultation with the Project Director of the Bank's Education Proj-
ect. 1/ The objective of the latter being that when these CESAO
courses are almost identical to the training component for
extension workers and managers of small projects under the Edu-
cation Project, most candidates would be selected from the Mossi
plateau ORDs not covered by the Education Project; when the courses
are different, the results will be compared with those obtained
under the Education Project.
After the proper selection procedures have been followed, the Director of
FDR would be responsible for the necessary follow-up action.
10.       Extension Study.  Matourkou provides training for about 100 ex-
tension workers annually, but has only limited staff. Some ORDs (report-
edly Ouagadougou, Kaya and Yatenga) have now organized their own training
programs for extension workers.  However, although output of extension
workers might be sufficient, from the narrow project point of view, there
are some doubts about quality of extension worker training and adequacy
of training facilities in relation to future overall manpower requirements.
Government is aware of this and has certain ideas on possible solutions
such as regionalizing Matourkou into three or four institutes. What is
needed however is a short study to pull the various elements and ideas
together and to provide a concise proposal for future action. This
could then be used to take decisions and if necessary form the basis to
approach aid agencies for necessary funding. Provision has been made under
the project for consultants to assist in making an extension training study.
Consultant(s) would be selected by the Ministry of Rural Development, but
the qualifications, terms of reference and terms of employment of these
consultants would be satisfactory to IDA. The aim would be to have the study
completed in 1977. For practical purposes the small disbursements involved
would be channeled through FDR.
1/   The Director of the Education Project is assisted by a Bureau of Pedagogy
which includes some 14 training specialists.

ANNEX 12
Page 1
UPPER VOLTA
SECOND RURAL DEVELOPMENT FUND PROJECT
Economic Benefits
1.        The proposed project basically provides a line of credit to FDR
for investment in small-scale scattered subprojects, to be selected by
FDR in accordance with procedures detailed in Annex 9. Although benefits
can be identified for individual subprojects, as has been done for typical
holdings in the.farm budgets in Annex 11, economic calculations can only
be tentative since most investment decisions upon which the rate of return
ultimately will depend, are yet to be made. For purposes of calculating
the cost of the Rural Development Fund, a development program (see Annex 4)
for works to be executed during the project period has been established.
Assuming that the mix of subprojects in this development program will
materialize, calculations have been made to assess the economic impact of
the line of credit of FDR.
2.        Assumptions in calculating this economie rate of return are as
follows:
(a) Project Life
The project life is assumed to be 20 years and there is no residual
value after that period.
(b) Project Costs
The costs are basically those shown in Annex 7, however, with the
following adjustments;
(i) price contingencies have been omitted;
(ii) identifiable taxes have been excluded;
(iii) the project component for unidentified subprojects has been
excluded;
(iv) the costs for studies for economic development of areas freed
from onchocerciasis have been excluded (see Annex 6 for their
justification);
(v) costs of extension services are not confined to the project
period, but have been extended over the life of the project
in accordance with a schedule of extension workers needed.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
Equipment and operating costs have also been extended over
the life of the project (at 5%) to reflect other overheads
mainly of ORDs needed to provide services to project
beneficiaries;
(vi) maintenance needs for tubewells are reflected in extending
the costs of the maintenance well brigade throughout the
life of the project; and
(vii) hired skilled labor used in construction is valued at the
various prevailing rates for the specific kind of labor
required, but freely provided farm and village labor 1/ has
not been valued, as this is provided during the dry season
when there are no other employment opportunities.
(c) Incremental and Maintenance Costs
Incremental on farm costs have been calculated based on incre-
mental physical input requirements, as shown in the farm budgets
for land and water development subprojects in Annex 11. For
bottomland and irrigation development incremental and total re-
quirements are identical. Incremental farm labor requirements
have been valued at CFAF 100/day since labor is required and
employed during the agricultural season. Labor is also required
for maintaining land and water development schemes and this
would be provided by the farmers, but since maintenance would be
executed during the dry season when there are no other employment
opportunities, maintenance labor has been valued at zero. The
involvement of technical services in maintenance of more complex
works has been reflected by CFAF 2,000 service charge/ha for im-
proved bottomland and small irrigation works.
(d) Benefit
(i) Estimated output from land and water development projects
are in Table 1. Outputs occur the year after investments
since calculations are based on project years (July 1 -
June 30) rather than calendar years.
1/   Farm and village labor has been valued at CFAF 100/day--the prevailing
rural payment for labor--during the agricultural season, when most rural
labor is fully employed. In the dry season, there are no employment
opportunities (except for some self-help schemes) and rural labor in this
period has not been valued. Two different rates for labor during differ-
ent seasons have been used rather than one wage rate reflecting an aver-
age, which would take employment opportunities in the various seasons
into account, to be used in the whole year.



ANNEX 12
Page 3
(ii)  Economic prices for project output are ,alculated in Tables
2 to 5 and are as follows:
Cereals              39 CFAF/kilo
Paddy                50 CFAF/kilo
Groundnuts           43 CFAF/kilo
Cotton               85 CFAF/kilo
Economic prices for cereals and paddy are based on import
substitution values, those for groundnuts and cotton on
export parity.
(iii) Project output from small irrigation projects is based on
paddy production. Vegetables could also be produced. Al-
though this would be more profitable, this has not been
taken into account because division in paddy and vegetable
output from these projects would be very arbitrary.
(iv) Benefits from agricultural credit are twofold: saved mandays
and acreage expansion. Based on an average equipment cost of
CFAF 65,000 about 1,700 farmers would benefit. Manday savings
are estimated at 80 days per holding. These have been valued
at CFAF 100, since savings occur during the agricultural sea-
son.  During that season, a family has sufficient alternative
agricultural work to employ the labor thus saved. Benefits
from acreage expansion (assumed ta be 2 ha--I ha under cotton
and 1 ha under groundnuts) are based on net economic benefits
with labor costed at CFAF 100.
(v) Benefits for wells and tubewells are based on the same meth-
odology as used for RDF I, 1/ updated to reflect latest prices.
3.        Based on the above assumptions, FDR's investment package would yield
a rate of return of 16%. Actual cost and benefit streams are in Table 6 and
sensitivity analysis results are in Table 7. The latter are not particularly
relevant because of the flexible nature of the Rural Development Fund, but
should provide some indication about shifts in the rate of return.
I/   See Report PA-127a, Appraisal of a Rural Development Fund Project Upper
Volta, dated May 16, 1972. In calculating these benefits, the following
was taken into account: additional available days because of improved
health (5 men in the village to work an additional 300 days at CFAF 100);
saved calories because reduced walking to water supply sources (60 fami-
lies at 16 km/day during 8 months - 230,000 km at 23 calories/km at 1 ton
cereals for 3.3 million calories saves 1.6 ton cereals); and additional
milk production from goats (2,400 liters at 12 CFAF).



ANNEX 12
Page 4
4.        If the benefits for project wells and tubewells, as quantified
above, are not taken into account into the project's benefit stream and if
as a consequence the related investments are deducted from project costs
(see Table 6 where the various items are marked), the remaining investment
package would yield a rate of return of 21% (see also Table 7).
5.        It should be remembered that the above economic rate of return
calculations fail to capture two important aspects; namely, (i) that the
main benefits accrue to the poorest segment of the Voltaic population
and mainly in an area with difficult ecological and human conditions; and
(ii) there is a direct effect on human well-being especially from invest-
ments that result in improved water supply for villagers. It should also
be remembered that, although the various subcomponents are not especially
risky provided they are constructed correctly and maintained properly, as
is common with all agricultural activity in Upper Volta, benefit levels are
liable to sharp fluctuations due to a notoriously variable rainfall.
6.        The project provides a line of credit which will be used for in-
vestment in various subprojects, some of which will be productive and have
benefits which can easily be quantified, while others will have social and
health benefits for which quantification is either difficult or impossible
and less useful. The principal economic benefits of the various subprojects
would be as follows:  bottomlands - increases in wet season rice production;
small-scale irrigation - increases in rice and season vegetable production;
erosion control and medium-term credit - increased production of cereals and
groundnuts; warehouses - reduction of storage losses in both farm inputs and
farm outputs, plus induced increases in input use; and wells and tubewells -
improved labor productivity and increased livestock production. The project
will also provide important social and institutional benefits. Wells give
an assured water supply to the human population, who otherwise depend on
small often distant creeks which run dry for at least three months of the
year; there is also a significant health impact as the creeks contain water-
borne diseases. Institutional strengthening has already occurred under the
first project by improving the capacity of FDR, HAER, and ORDs to sponsor
and implement sound development. The proposed strengthening of these insti-
tutions under the project would further improve their development capacity.
7.        As explained above economic calculations are tentative because
the project provides basically a line of credit to FDR. Therefore, separate
rate of returns for various subcomponents are even more tentative and less
useful (except to a certain extent for ranking) especially since it would
be highly arbitrary to allocate overheads and to make final assumptions
about the annual pace of development and execution of works under various
subcomponents. However, to give an impression about ranking, based on
simple models using the development program in Annex 4, and assuming that
the benefits described in the farm budgets (Annex 11) will materialize
without allocating overheads to the various subcomponents, rates of return



ANNEX 12
Page 5
for the various productive components of the project would be as follows:
bottomlands, over 50%; erosion control, 30%; improved bottomlands, 18%;
small-scale irrigation, 16%; and agricultural equipment, over 50%. If
the same assumptions and benefits described in the footnote on page 3 are
used, the well and tubewell component will have a rate of return of 6%.
The latter indicates already that there are more than only quantifiable
economic considerations (health, farmer response, availability of re-
sources to be developed, etc.) which determine the selection of subproj-
ects to be financed by RDF.
8.        Overall it has been demonstrated that an investment package like
the one proposed under this project will yield economic benefits for Upper
Volta. It is also clear that the various subcomponents which have been
identified are justified either on economic or on social grounds or on a
combination of both. For projects not yet identified, appraisal by FDR will
include economic justification, and this will also be done in studies for
small-scale irrigation and improved bottomlands (see also Annex 9). Further-
more, evaluation will involve, from time to time, the verification of the
various subprojects' justification.

/ Value of seed not included. Official price (used in farm budgets) is CFAF 35/kilo.
For economic calculations a price of CFAF 85/kilo has been used.
